# M09 Phase 9 — Cluster Synthesis — structural input

**Date:** 2026-05-22
**Cluster:** M09 — Humility, Meekness and Submission

---

## §1 — Cluster overview

M09 (Humility, Meekness and Submission) holds 6 inner-being characteristics:

| Char | Short name | One-line definition |
|---:|---|---|
| 1 | Humility | The inner disposition of voluntary self-lowering before God and others. |
| 2 | Submission | The inner disposition of willing yielding to legitimate authority — heart-located submission of will, not merely external compliance. |
| 3 | Contrition | The acute form of humility produced by genuine confrontation with one's failure or with the holiness of God — crushed/broken spirit. |
| 4 | Meekness and gentleness | The inner quality of calibrated restraint and gentleness — controlled strength, capacity to hold force in measured response. |
| 5 | Dignity | Grounded moral gravity and seriousness of character — inner-grounded worth that does NOT require self-promotion or status-seeking. |
| 6 | Willing-heartedness | The freely-moved willing disposition of the heart/spirit. |

Each characteristic produced 189 findings in its Phase 9 batch (1,323 rows total in `cluster_finding`).

---

## §2 — Confirmed cluster observations (with resolution notes)

These four observations were raised at characteristic-mapping time and confirmed during Phase 9. They name the boundaries already validated and are direct inputs to the synthesis.

### Observation #16 — INTEGRATION_NOTE (linked to Char 19)

**Title:** CHAR-1 volume-split across M09-A / M09-B

**Description:**

> CHAR-1 (Humility) was volume-split per v2_8 §8.6: M09-A (37V willed/voluntary self-lowering) + M09-B (13V state/imposed lowliness). The characteristic identity persists across the split; Phase 9 evaluates CHAR-1 as a single inner-being faculty manifesting in two registers (the voluntary disposition vs the experienced state). The split-axis is voluntary-vs-imposed.

---

### Observation #17 — INTER_RELATIONSHIP (linked to Char 20)

**Title:** M09 submission ↔ M30 (Obedience) register-adjacency

**Description:**

> CHAR-2 (Submission) is register-adjacent with the future M30 (Obedience/Disobedience) cluster. M09-C/D hupakoē/hupakouō/hupotagē/yiq.qe.hah carry the submission-of-will register; M30 will carry the broader obedience-as-action register. When M30 opens, M09-C/D evidence will inform M30's T6 relational findings. Cross-register flag carried forward.

---

### Observation #18 — INTER_RELATIONSHIP (linked to Char 23)

**Title:** M09 dignity ↔ M08 pride as structural opposites

**Description:**

> CHAR-5 (Dignity, semnotēs) functions as the structural opposite of M08 (Pride) — grounded moral gravity vs proud self-display. The 3 semnotēs verses (1Ti 2:2, 1Ti 3:4, Tit 2:7) name the inner-grounded worth that does not require self-promotion. Phase 9 T6 should articulate the M09-G ↔ M08 polarity.

---

### Observation #19 — INTER_RELATIONSHIP (linked to Char 24)

**Title:** M09 willing-heartedness ↔ M04 joy + M29 desire/will

**Description:**

> CHAR-6 (Willing-heartedness, na.div) overlaps with M04 (joy/delight — spontaneous generosity dimension) and M29 (desire/will — volitional readiness dimension). Psa 51:12 in particular pairs willing spirit with joy of salvation. When M04 / M29 open, the na.div corpus will inform their cross-cluster T6 findings.

---

**Title:** M23 pickup note: G1299 diatassō (11 verses set-aside at Phase 8.5)

**Description:**

> At Phase 8.5 (2026-05-22), 11 G1299 diatassō verses (Mat 11:1, Luk 3:13, Luk 17:9, Act 7:44, Act 18:2, 1Cor 7:17, 1Cor 9:14, 1Cor 11:34, 1Cor 16:1, Gal 3:19, Tit 1:5) were set_aside from M09 because the verses evidence practical command-giving / narrative direction — no M09 inner-disposition content. Pass A meanings explicitly note "does not evidence inner-being content for this term." The term's primary register is M23 (authority/directive). One diatassō verse (Luk 17:10) carried M09-relational content and was kept in M09-C. WHEN M23 OPENS: the 11 set-aside verses are candidates for adoption as M23 evidence (the term mti_id stays in M09 because Luk 17:10 evidences M09; M23 picks up the verses via cross-cluster co-reference).

**Description:**

M09-D-VCG-05 (the dedicated PHASE_8_5_FLAG VCG holding the 11 diatassō verses) was soft-deleted post-set-aside. Substantive corpus: 109 → 97 is_relevant verses; M09-D 30 → 19 verses.

## §3 — Per-prompt synthesis matrix

For each of the 189 prompts (T0.1.1 … T7.3.4), the 6 characteristics' findings are stacked verbatim below. Read all 5 before authoring the cluster-scope row in your output.

### T0

#### T0.1 — Divine Nature Reflected

##### T0.1.1 — What does the verse evidence reveal about the nature or character of God that this characteristic reflects or images?

**[CHAR-1] Humility** (E)

The corpus reveals that God himself is the perceiver and responder to humility in the inner person, suggesting that humility corresponds to something in the divine nature that recognises and honours it. In the ka.na evidence, God repeatedly observes the inner act of humbling (2Ch12:7 — "they have humbled themselves; I will not destroy them"), responds to it with mercy (1Ki21:29; 2Ch12:12; 2Ch34:27), and explicitly contrasts it with proud self-assertion as the posture he opposes (Jam4:6 — "God opposes the proud but gives grace to the humble"; 1Pe5:5). Most significantly, Christ's self-humbling (Phili2:8 — "he humbled himself by becoming obedient to the point of death") and his own self-description as "gentle and lowly in heart" (Mat11:29) place humility within the divine character itself. The characteristic thus reflects a God who, in the Incarnation, voluntarily takes the downward path — suggesting humility is not merely a creaturely virtue but an expression of the divine self-giving nature.

**[CHAR-2] Submission** (E)

The corpus reveals God as a sovereign authority who requires and receives the inner yielding of the will from persons he has created and redeemed. The gospel-obedience cluster (M09-D-VCG-01 — Rom1:5; Rom16:26; Rom10:16; 2Th1:8) frames obedience of faith as the telos of divine disclosure — the mystery now revealed demands and elicits the will's submission. God's character is thus that of one who does not merely command external behaviour but seeks the inner allegiance of the will ("obedience of faith" — Rom1:5; "the will's trustful submission as the culminating response to revealed mystery" — Rom16:26). Rom5:19 adds the Christological dimension: God's own Son enacts the obedience that makes many righteous — implying that submission of will is not merely imposed on creatures but expressed by the Son in his relation to the Father.

**[CHAR-3] Contrition** (E)

Both verses locate God's response to contrition as nearness, saving action, and indwelling presence: Psa34:18 — "the Lord is near to the brokenhearted and saves the crushed in spirit"; Isa57:15 — "I dwell in the high and holy place, and also with him who is of a contrite and lowly spirit, to revive the spirit of the lowly, and to revive the heart of the contrite." God is revealed as one who is drawn toward the crushed inner state rather than repelled by it — his nearness and dwelling are characteristically responsive to contrition. The character of God evidenced is that of one who inhabits both transcendence ("the high and holy place") and the broken interior of the contrite person — suggesting that contrition is the inner state most aligned with God's dwelling-presence.

**[CHAR-4] Meekness and gentleness** (E)

Heb5:2 — the high priest who "can deal gently with the ignorant and wayward, since he himself is beset with weakness" — images a God who, in the institution of the Aaronic priesthood, designed a mediator whose gentleness is grounded in shared vulnerability. The high priest is the image-bearing mediator who brings God's responsive compassion to the erring; his metriopatheō images the divine disposition toward the wayward. The verse implies that God's own disposition toward the ignorant and erring is one of measured, compassionate restraint rather than severity — the high priest's capacity to "deal gently" reflects a priestly-mediating access to the divine gentle-toward-erring character. Act9:38 evidences the inner-volitional readiness (absence of hesitation) as an expected quality of the community's messenger — imaging the responsive character of those sent in God's name.

**[CHAR-5] Dignity** (E)

All three verses pair semnotēs with godliness-oriented life or authenticating character: 1Ti2:2 — "godly and dignified in every way"; Tit2:7 — "integrity, dignity" as teaching-character qualities. The pairing with godliness (eusebeia — God-directed life) in 1Ti2:2 suggests that dignity images the weighty seriousness of the divine character — God's own moral gravity and holiness are reflected in the sober, non-self-promoting gravitas that semnotēs names. The characteristic images the aspect of God's character that is grounded, serious, and not given to display or performance — the settled moral weight that belongs to holiness without ostentation.

**[CHAR-6] Willing-heartedness** (E)

The corpus reveals God as one who both calls for and restores the freely-moved willing spirit. Exo35:5 — "take from among you a contribution to the Lord" — God calls for an offering that must originate in a willing heart: he does not require coerced contribution but freely-given offering from the heart's genuine motivation. 2Ch29:31 — "all who were of a willing heart brought burnt offerings" — the context of restored worship after Hezekiah's reformation images God as delighted in freely-offered devotion. Most significantly, Psa51:12 — "uphold me with a willing spirit" — evidences God as the one who restores and sustains the willing spirit after moral failure: God is the giver and sustainer of the very inner quality he calls for. The characteristic thus images a God who desires genuine, uncoerced inner offering rather than external compliance, and who actively works to restore the inner capacity for freely-given devotion.

---

##### T0.1.2 — Does Scripture explicitly attribute this characteristic to God — and if so, what does that attribution reveal about its significance in the human person?

**[CHAR-1] Humility** (E)

Scripture attributes humility to the incarnate Son explicitly and personally: Jesus names himself "gentle and lowly in heart" (Mat11:29) and the Phili2:8 passage depicts his self-humbling as a willed, downward movement of the self taking on creaturely form and accepting death. This attribution is remarkable: the characteristic most often associated with creaturely dependence is owned by the one who has no need of dependence. That the Son takes on humility voluntarily rather than by necessity elevates the characteristic's significance — in the human person, humility is not merely an appropriate response to creatureliness but a participation in the self-giving movement that characterises the Son's own inner life. The Phili2 context frames the human call to humility (v.3 — "count others more significant") explicitly as imitation of Christ's pattern (v.5–8), establishing a direct connexion between the divine attribution and the human calling.

**[CHAR-2] Submission** (E)

The corpus attributes submission explicitly to Christ: Heb5:8 — "although he was a son, he learned obedience through what he suffered" — the incarnate Son experiences submission as something acquired through costly suffering. Rom5:19 — Christ's obedience as the redemptive act that makes many righteous — positions the Son's will-submission as the structural pivot of salvation history. The attribution to Christ reveals that submission of will is not a deficit forced on the creature by divine power but a posture the Son himself takes in relation to the Father. In the human person, the characteristic therefore carries Christological dignity: the one who submits participates in the inner-relational posture of the Son toward the Father, not merely in a social compliance structure.

**[CHAR-3] Contrition** (S)

The corpus does not explicitly attribute contrition to God. In both verses, God is the responsive agent — the one who draws near, saves, and revives — rather than the one who is contrite. The characteristic is exclusively creaturely in its direct attribution within this corpus: contrition is the inner state of the person; God is the one who responds to it with saving nearness.

**[CHAR-4] Meekness and gentleness** (E)

The corpus does not explicitly name God as possessing metriopatheō or okneō. However, Heb5:2 is part of the Hebrews argument leading to Christ as the superior high priest (Heb4:15 — "we do not have a high priest who is unable to sympathize with our weaknesses" — outside corpus but the structural context). The implication is that Christ's compassionate gentle-dealing (the antitype of the Aaronic priest's metriopatheō) reflects the divine character toward erring humanity. The attribution is indirect but structurally present: the Christological high priest carries this characteristic as a quality of the divine mediator.

**[CHAR-5] Dignity** (S)

The three-verse corpus does not explicitly attribute semnotēs to God. The characteristic is named as a quality of human persons (believers in 1Ti2:2, the overseer in 1Ti3:4, Titus in Tit2:7). The pairing with godliness (1Ti2:2) implies an orientation toward God but does not directly attribute semnotēs to him.

**[CHAR-6] Willing-heartedness** (S)

The five-verse corpus does not explicitly attribute willing-heartedness to God. In all five verses, the characteristic is a quality of human persons (the willing-hearted givers in Exo35; the willing craftsmen in 1Ch28:21; the penitent psalmist in Psa51:12). God is the recipient and restorer of the characteristic rather than its primary bearer. The implicit direction of attribution is that God values and can restore the characteristic, but is not described as having it himself.

---

##### T0.1.3 — Where Scripture is silent about God's possession of this characteristic, what does that silence suggest about the characteristic's place in the divine image?

**[CHAR-1] Humility** (E)

The evidence is not silent on this matter (see T0.1.2). However, where the corpus addresses humility in the Father rather than the Son, it is consistently indirect: God *responds to* humility, *requires* humility (Mic6:8 — "what does the Lord require of you but… to walk humbly with your God"), and *produces* humility in the person. The Father's own possessing of the characteristic is not directly asserted in the corpus — only the Son's. This asymmetry suggests the characteristic, while present in the divine life, takes its definitively creaturely form in the Incarnation: the form in which a being descends below its natural standing, which is structurally unavailable to the Father in the same way. The silence at the Father level is analytically significant: humility in the human person is imago Dei not by simple reflection of the Father's nature but by participation in the Son's kenotic movement.

**[CHAR-2] Submission** (E)

The corpus does not evidence the Father possessing submission in the sense the Son does — the Father is consistently the one to whom Christ submits (Heb5:8 — "in submission to the Father"; the gospel as his command in Rom16:26). This asymmetry — Son submits, Father receives — suggests that submission in its creaturely form is specifically the Son's characteristic as the one who takes on creaturely relation with the Father in the Incarnation. For the human person, the implication is that submission reflects the image not by mirroring the Father's posture but by participating in the Son's: the human creature before God occupies the position the Son voluntarily occupies, and the characteristic is thus genuinely image-bearing through union with the one who exercises it fully.

**[CHAR-3] Contrition** (E)

The silence on God's own contrition is analytically significant. Contrition is structurally the inner state of one who has confronted their own failure or the holiness of another — a posture that presupposes moral limitation or transgression. God's possession of this characteristic in its creaturely form would be constitutionally incoherent. The silence therefore reveals that contrition is an exclusively creaturely characteristic — uniquely belonging to the moral creature who exists in the tension between their own failure and God's holiness. It does not image God by mirroring a divine quality but by being the inner state through which the creature's moral reality is honestly confronted before God.

**[CHAR-4] Meekness and gentleness** (E)

The two-verse corpus is primarily silent on direct divine attribution of meekness or gentleness to God himself. In Heb5:2, the high priest's gentleness is specifically grounded in his own shared creaturely weakness — a structural ground that does not apply to God in the same form. The silence suggests that meekness/gentleness in its specifically creaturely form (the capacity for measured restraint grounded in one's own vulnerability and limitation) is a uniquely creaturely characteristic — it is the image-bearing expression of something in God (compassionate restraint toward the erring) but its specific form (grounded in shared weakness) is exclusively creaturely and characterises the mediating human figure.

**[CHAR-5] Dignity** (E)

The silence on divine attribution suggests that semnotēs in its creaturely form is the image-bearer's participation in the quality of settled moral gravity that belongs properly to God's holiness, expressed in the form appropriate to creaturely existence. The dignity of the human person before God is the creaturely expression of the divine weightiness — not God's own attribute directly reflected but the creature's share in the ordering gravity that characterises created existence properly ordered toward God. The silence also reflects that semnotēs has a distinctly social-characterological expression (visible in public life, household governance, teaching) that requires the creaturely social context for its expression.

**[CHAR-6] Willing-heartedness** (E)

The silence on divine possession suggests that willing-heartedness in its creaturely form is specifically the inner posture of the creature *toward God* — the freely-moved heart offering to the one who calls. The characteristic presupposes an asymmetry: there is one who calls (God) and one who freely offers (the creature). God cannot be willing-hearted toward himself in the same structural sense; the characteristic is constitutively creaturely in its directional structure. However, the divine calling for free offering rather than coerced contribution (Exo35:5) and God's own free giving (Psa51:12 — upholding with a willing spirit) suggest that God's own liberality is the divine ground from which the creaturely willing-heartedness derives and which it reflects: the freely-giving God calls for freely-giving creatures.

---

#### T0.2 — Created Purpose

##### T0.2.1 — What does the verse evidence suggest about the purpose this characteristic serves in the human person as created — what does it equip the person to be, do, or become?

**[CHAR-1] Humility** (E)

The corpus evidences several purposes humility serves in the human person. First, it equips the person for genuine covenant relation with God: across the ka.na evidence (2Ch7:14; 2Ki22:19; 2Ch34:27), humbling oneself is the inner act that restores or maintains the covenantal relationship — it positions the person receptively before God's word and action. Second, it equips the person for wisdom: Pro11:2 — "with the humble is wisdom" — establishes humility as the inner disposition that opens the person to true understanding rather than closing them in self-sufficiency. Third, it equips the person for genuine community: Eph4:2 places humility as foundational to the corporate harmony of the body; Phili2:3 defines it as the cognitive reordering that enables genuine regard for others. Fourth, it is the inner posture through which God's reversal-work operates: Luk18:14 (the tax collector justified) and the exaltation-of-the-humble pattern evidence humility as the condition through which divine vindication moves.

**[CHAR-2] Submission** (E)

The corpus evidences several purposes. First, submission equips the person to receive salvation: Heb5:9 — "he became the source of eternal salvation to all who obey him" — the obedient will is the condition for receiving Christ's benefit. Second, it equips the person to be formed in character: Heb5:8 — obedience learned through suffering is a quality shaped over time; 1Pe1:22 — obedience to truth "purifies the soul itself," producing sincere love from a pure heart. Third, it equips the person for genuine community: household obedience patterns (Eph6:1; Col3:20; 1Ti3:4) show submission as the disposition that enables ordered, functioning human structures. Fourth, it equips the person for covenant fidelity: the gospel-obedience cluster (Rom1:5; 16:26) frames obedience of faith as the telos of the person's reception of the gospel — what they are becoming through it.

**[CHAR-3] Contrition** (E)

Both verses evidence contrition as the inner condition that positions the person for divine encounter and restoration. Psa34:18 — God's nearness and saving action is the direct response to the crushed spirit; the purpose of contrition is to create the interior openness through which saving action enters. Isa57:15 — God's dwelling with and reviving of the contrite spirit identifies the characteristic's purpose as enabling divine indwelling and the reviving of spirit and heart. The characteristic equips the person to receive what God gives — saving nearness, reviving presence — by creating the inner poverty that does not resist or deflect divine action. Its purpose is receptive: making the person capable of receiving what pride and self-sufficiency prevent.

**[CHAR-4] Meekness and gentleness** (E)

Heb5:2 evidences the purpose most directly: the high priest's metriopatheō equips him to mediate between the holy God and the "ignorant and wayward" — to be the point of compassionate contact for those who have erred. Without this characteristic, the mediator would relate to the erring with severity or detachment rather than measured compassionate restraint; the characteristic equips the priest for effective mediating ministry. Act9:38 evidences a second purpose: okneō (unimpeded responsiveness) equips the person to be responsive to genuine need without the friction of hesitation — equipping for effective service and responsiveness to others' situations.

**[CHAR-5] Dignity** (E)

The three verses evidence three distinct purposes. First, semnotēs equips the person for ordered public life: 1Ti2:2 — dignity as the inner quality characterising a "peaceful and quiet life" — the characteristic enables the person to inhabit public existence with composure rather than self-promotional instability. Second, semnotēs equips the leader for effective household governance: 1Ti3:4 — "with all dignity keeping his children submissive" — the characteristic is the inner quality from which ordered authority flows; it equips the person to lead without requiring force. Third, semnotēs equips the teacher for authentic instruction: Tit2:7 — dignity alongside integrity as the qualities that authenticate teaching; without gravitas, instruction lacks the character-ground that makes it credible.

**[CHAR-6] Willing-heartedness** (E)

The corpus evidences three purposes. First, willing-heartedness equips the person for *genuine worship and offering*: Exo35:5 and 35:22 — the freely-moved heart is the inner condition that produces genuine contribution to the Lord's service rather than compelled donation. Second, it equips the person for *wholehearted skilled service*: 1Ch28:21 — willingness coupled with skill enables wholehearted participation in the temple work. Third, Psa51:12 — the willing spirit as the condition for sustaining "joyful, freely-offered devotion" — equips the person for sustained devoted engagement with God after moral failure. Together: the characteristic equips the person to give and serve genuinely rather than under compulsion, and to sustain that giving orientation over time.

---

##### T0.2.2 — Does the evidence indicate whether this characteristic is part of the original created design, a response to the fallen condition, or both?

**[CHAR-1] Humility** (E)

The evidence suggests humility operates in both registers, though the corpus is weighted toward the response-to-fallen-condition side. The ka.na evidence is almost entirely set within contexts of moral failure, covenant violation, and divine judgment — humbling before God is the required inner response when the people have sinned (2Ch7:14; 2Ch12:6; 2Ch33:12; Lev26:41). Similarly, the tapeinoō inversion pattern (Mat23:12; Luk18:14) addresses those who have exalted themselves wrongly. This strongly positions humility as the corrective posture of the fallen inner person. However, Mic6:8 ("walk humbly with your God") and the portrait of Christ's self-humbling (Phili2:8) point toward humility as a structural feature of right creaturely-to-Creator relation — not merely corrective but constitutive of what it means to be a creature before God. Phili2:3's call to count others more significant, framed as the character-garment of the renewed person (Col3:12), suggests humility belongs to the created design that the fall disrupts and renewal restores.

**[CHAR-2] Submission** (E)

The evidence points to both but with different emphases in the two sub-groups. The M09-C heart/conscience-located evidence is largely addressed to persons already in the redemptive situation (believers — Rom6:12/17; Eph6:5; Phili2:12; Col3:22), suggesting submission as the renewed-person's characteristic response to their condition. However, Gen49:10 (yiq.qe.hah — the peoples' obedience to Judah's coming ruler) frames submission as an eschatological ordering rather than a fallen-condition response. Pro30:17 (obedience to parents as expected natural moral response) suggests submission to legitimate authority is part of created moral order. Heb5:8 (Christ learning obedience through suffering) implies submission is not automatic even for the Son — it is something developed through engagement with hardship, suggesting it belongs to the creaturely-relational structure, not only the fallen-remedial register.

**[CHAR-3] Contrition** (E)

The corpus strongly evidences contrition as a response to the fallen condition rather than part of original design. Both verses operate in the context of moral failure, limitation, and the recognition of one's true standing before God's holiness: Psa34:18 — "the brokenhearted" and "crushed in spirit" — language that presupposes a condition of inner breaking. Isa57:15 — "contrite" paired with "lowly" — the characteristic arises from genuine confrontation with failure or with divine holiness. There is no evidence in this corpus that contrition exists in the absence of the moral condition (failure, sin, guilt) that occasions it. The characteristic appears structurally responsive to the fallen condition: it is the right inner response to one's own moral reality before a holy God.

**[CHAR-4] Meekness and gentleness** (E)

Heb5:2's ground for gentleness is shared creaturely weakness ("since he himself is beset with weakness") — a condition that the fallen order intensifies but that has creaturely roots in finitude and limitation even apart from sin. The characteristic is thus positioned as a response to the creaturely condition of limitation and waywardness, which is itself intensified by the fallen condition. The gentleness that responds to waywardness (Heb5:2) is specifically a response to the fallen condition; the volitional readiness of okneō (Act9:38) may be more directly a quality of created relational responsiveness. The evidence is mixed: partly original-creaturely (readiness) and partly fallen-condition-responsive (gentleness toward the wayward).

**[CHAR-5] Dignity** (E)

The evidence positions the characteristic as belonging primarily to the order of creation and the renewed community rather than as specifically a response to the fallen condition. 1Ti2:2's "peaceful and quiet life, godly and dignified in every way" is the characteristic of well-ordered existence rather than specifically a corrective to sin. 1Ti3:4's household order through dignity and Tit2:7's teaching authenticity are ordering qualities that belong to human social existence as designed. The characteristic's specific expression (maintaining dignity under governance, Tit2:7, 1Ti3:4) may be intensified by the fallen condition but is constitutively an ordering quality of human social existence.

**[CHAR-6] Willing-heartedness** (E)

The evidence suggests both registers. Exo35:5/22 and 2Ch29:31 — the freely-moving willing heart producing generous offering and worship — evidence the characteristic as a positive, creation-consistent orientation of the human will toward God; there is no explicit fallen-condition framing in these verses. 1Ch28:21 — willingness enabling skilled service — similarly addresses the characteristic as a positive given rather than a remedial response. However, Psa51:12 is explicitly post-failure: "uphold me with a willing spirit" is the prayer of the penitent who has lost the willing orientation through moral failure. This evidences the characteristic as both an original-design orientation (the freely-given heart) and one that must be restored after the fallen condition's effects (Psa51:12 — restoration sought after failure).

---

##### T0.2.3 — Is there evidence that this characteristic is oriented toward a future fullness — something the person is moving toward, not only what they currently are?

**[CHAR-1] Humility** (E)

The exaltation-pattern provides the primary eschatological orientation: Mat18:4, Mat23:12, Luk14:11, Luk18:14, Jam4:10, 1Pe5:6 all link the present willed self-lowering to a future divine exaltation ("he will exalt you" — 1Pe5:6; "at the proper time he may exalt you" — 1Pe5:6). The present humility is explicitly the condition for a future vindication not yet realised. Phili3:21 adds a creaturely-eschatological dimension: the present "lowly body" (tapeinōsis) awaits transformation into conformity with Christ's glorious body — the experienced lowliness of the creaturely state is itself oriented toward a future glorification. The characteristic is thus doubly eschatological: the voluntary self-lowering of M09-A points toward divine exaltation, and the experienced creaturely lowliness of M09-B points toward bodily transformation.

**[CHAR-2] Submission** (E)

Yes. The obedience-of-faith pattern in Rom1:5 and Rom16:26 frames the characteristic as the goal toward which Paul's mission presses — a future fullness of the nations' will-submission to God. Gen49:10 explicitly eschatological: "to him shall be the obedience of the peoples" — the gathering of nations in submission to Judah's coming ruler is a future-oriented gathering. Heb5:9 — obedience as the condition for receiving salvation from the one "made perfect" — places the characteristic within the eschatological-salvific frame. 1Pe1:2 — obedience as the goal of the Spirit's sanctifying work — frames the Spirit's entire sanctifying activity as oriented toward the fullness of this inner posture. The characteristic thus has a telos: the full and genuine submission of the creature's will to Christ as Lord, which the present obedience of faith partially anticipates.

**[CHAR-3] Contrition** (E)

Yes, indirectly. Isa57:15's "revive the spirit of the lowly, and to revive the heart of the contrite" points toward a renewal and restoration — the contrite state is not a permanent condition but one through which God moves toward reviving. The reviving suggests a future state of greater vitality, not permanent brokenness. However, whether this reviving itself represents an eschatological orientation or a this-life restoration is not specified in the two-verse corpus. The evidence is limited to the trajectory of God's reviving response to contrition rather than an explicit eschatological telos.

**[CHAR-4] Meekness and gentleness** (S)

The two-verse corpus does not directly evidence an eschatological orientation for this characteristic. Act9:38 addresses an immediate pastoral situation; Heb5:2 addresses the present mediating function of the high priest. The broader Hebrews argument points toward Christ's superior priestly role (Heb4:15 — outside corpus), which has eschatological dimensions, but within the two-verse evidence of §3.C this eschatological trajectory is not directly stated.

**[CHAR-5] Dignity** (S)

The three-verse corpus does not directly evidence an eschatological orientation for semnotēs. The characteristic is addressed to present community existence (1Ti2:2; 1Ti3:4; Tit2:7) without explicit eschatological framing. The characteristic's function as an ordering quality of present social existence does not carry a direct eschatological trajectory in this evidence.

**[CHAR-6] Willing-heartedness** (S)

The five-verse corpus does not directly evidence an eschatological orientation for willing-heartedness. The Exodus and Chronicles contexts address present community worship and service; Psa51:12 addresses present penitential restoration. No explicit eschatological framing appears in §3.E.

---

#### T0.3 — Image-Bearer Expression

##### T0.3.1 — In what way does this characteristic express the divine image in the human person — what aspect of being made in God's likeness does it instantiate?

**[CHAR-1] Humility** (E)

Humility expresses the divine image most directly through the imitation of the Son's kenotic self-giving. The Phili2 passage uses Christ's self-humbling (v.8) as the explicit ground for the human call to humility (v.3 — "in humility count others more significant"), making the connexion between the divine image-prototype and the human image-bearer structural. The characteristic instantiates the aspect of the divine likeness that involves self-giving rather than self-assertion: God-in-Christ moves downward for the sake of others; the image-bearer reflects this in counting others more significant and voluntarily taking the lower place (Mat18:4; Phili2:3). More broadly, humility instantiates the creaturely capacity for right ordered relation to the Creator — the openness to God's word, authority, and reversal that constitutes the image-bearer as genuinely responsive rather than self-enclosed.

**[CHAR-2] Submission** (E)

Submission expresses the divine image through the pattern of the Son's own inner life: the human creature's willing yield of its will to God mirrors the Son's submission to the Father (Heb5:8; Rom5:19). The characteristic instantiates the aspect of the divine image that involves relational responsiveness to legitimate authority — the image-bearer is made to be a creature in genuine relation with the Creator, and submission is the inner form that relation takes. 1Pe1:2 ("in the sanctification of the Spirit, for obedience to Jesus Christ") positions the Spirit's work as directed toward producing this image-quality in the person: being progressively shaped into the posture of willing yield to Christ.

**[CHAR-3] Contrition** (E)

Contrition expresses the divine image not by mirroring a divine quality but by enabling the human person's genuine relational encounter with God at the point of moral reality. Both verses evidence God's nearness to the contrite person (Psa34:18; Isa57:15) — the characteristic is the inner state that makes genuine divine-human encounter possible at the deepest level of the person's moral condition. The image is expressed through the person's capacity for honest self-confrontation before God's holiness — a capacity that only the being made in God's image can exercise. The characteristic thus expresses the image-bearing capacity for genuine moral self-knowledge and genuine relation with the holy God.

**[CHAR-4] Meekness and gentleness** (E)

Heb5:2 evidences the characteristic expressing the divine image through the mediating-compassion dimension: the high priest who can "deal gently with the ignorant and wayward" images the divine disposition of reaching toward the erring rather than abandoning or condemning them. The image is expressed through the person's capacity for *measured compassionate restraint* — the inner quality that holds the capacity to judge or correct while choosing to engage with gentleness grounded in shared vulnerability. This instantiates the aspect of the divine image that is characterised by compassionate mediation rather than severity. Act9:38 expresses the image through *responsive readiness* — the person who is immediately and freely responsive to genuine need, without hesitation or self-protective withdrawal.

**[CHAR-5] Dignity** (E)

The characteristic expresses the divine image through the quality of settled moral gravity that does not need self-promotion or status-assertion — the image-bearer who possesses genuine semnotēs reflects the ordered, non-anxious weightiness that belongs to the divine character without performance or display. 1Ti2:2's "godly and dignified in every way" positions the characteristic as the outward expression of inward godliness — the image-bearer's dignity is the creaturely form of the gravity that characterises the divine holiness when it inhabits a human life.

**[CHAR-6] Willing-heartedness** (E)

Willing-heartedness expresses the divine image through the *freely-giving dimension* — the creature's uncoerced, internally-motivated generosity toward God reflects the divine freedom and liberality. God does not give because compelled but freely; the willing-hearted creature similarly gives freely rather than under constraint. Exo35:5/22 — "whoever is of a generous/willing heart" — the non-compelled offering images the divine liberality in creaturely form. Psa51:12 adds the dimension of willing spirit as what enables joyful freely-offered devotion — the image-bearing creature at its most expressive of the divine generous character is the one whose spirit is willing rather than dragged.

---

##### T0.3.2 — Does the evidence suggest that this characteristic is shared between God and the human person, or is it exclusively a creaturely analogue to something in God?

**[CHAR-1] Humility** (E)

The evidence positions humility as partially shared and partially creaturely-analogue. The shared dimension: Christ's self-humbling (Phili2:8; Mat11:29) establishes that the divine Son takes on the characteristic genuinely, not merely analogically. The creaturely-analogue dimension: the specific form of humility as response to one's own sin, limitation, or dependence (the dominant register in the ka.na corpus and the inversion pattern) is structurally unavailable to God. What is shared is the self-giving, downward movement — what the Father enacts in sending the Son and what the Son enacts in his kenosis. What is uniquely creaturely is the form of humility that names one's own failure, dependence, and need before a greater power. The characteristic thus has both a genuinely shared dimension (the kenotic movement) and a creaturely-analogue dimension (the creature's appropriate posture before Creator).

**[CHAR-2] Submission** (E)

Partially shared through the Son's Incarnation, not directly shared in the Father's mode. Heb5:8 and Rom5:19 evidence that the characteristic is genuinely present in the Son — not merely a creaturely analogue but the Son's own enacted posture in the Incarnation. However, the Father's possession of the characteristic in the same form is not evidenced — the Father is the authority to whom submission runs, not the one who submits. The characteristic is thus shared with the person of the Son in his incarnate mode, and is a creaturely analogue to the divine-Son relation with the Father, which the Incarnation makes directly accessible as a pattern for human submission.

**[CHAR-3] Contrition** (E)

The evidence positions contrition as exclusively creaturely (see T0.1.2 and T0.1.3). There is no divine possession of the characteristic in this corpus — only divine responsiveness to it. Contrition is a uniquely creaturely phenomenon that presupposes the moral structure of a being capable of failure before a holy God. It is not a creaturely analogue to something in God but a uniquely creaturely inner-being state that God responds to with nearness, saving action, and reviving.

**[CHAR-4] Meekness and gentleness** (E)

The evidence positions the characteristic as primarily creaturely in its specific form but imaging a divine quality. Heb5:2's ground for the high priest's gentleness — shared creaturely weakness — is specifically creaturely. However, the quality of compassionate measured restraint toward the erring that the high priest exercises images what the divine character does through mediation. The characteristic is thus a creaturely analogue to a divine quality (compassionate gentleness toward the wayward) in a form specific to creaturely limitation and shared vulnerability.

**[CHAR-5] Dignity** (E)

The evidence positions semnotēs as a creaturely expression of a divine quality rather than a directly shared characteristic. The three verses address the characteristic entirely in human contexts (public life, household leadership, teaching) without attributing it to God. The characteristic is the creaturely image-bearing expression of the divine moral gravity and seriousness — the human person's participation in the weightiness that belongs to God's holiness, in the form appropriate to creaturely social existence.

**[CHAR-6] Willing-heartedness** (E)

The characteristic has a creaturely form (the creature's freely-moved will toward God) that images the divine liberality but is not directly shared in the same structural way. God's liberality is his own essential character; the creature's willing-heartedness is the responsive imaging of that liberality in the creaturely direction (from creature toward God). The structural asymmetry (God as recipient and sustainer; creature as willing giver) confirms the characteristic as primarily creaturely in its specific form, imaging a divine quality without being structurally identical to it.

---

##### T0.3.3 — What does the presence or absence of this characteristic in a person reveal about the condition of the divine image in that person?

**[CHAR-1] Humility** (E)

The corpus is explicit: the absence of humility is consistently linked to inner deterioration, moral failure, and divine opposition. 2Ch33:23 frames Amon's failure to humble himself as directly linked to increasing guilt; 2Ch36:12 identifies Zedekiah's refusal as the inner root of his evil; Psa107:12 evidences God actively bowing hearts down when the voluntary posture is absent — divine imposition replacing what the person will not offer. The structural contrast in Pro11:2 ("pride comes → disgrace; with the humble → wisdom") shows the absence of humility as the presence of pride — the distortion of the image most directly opposed to the divine self-giving nature. Conversely, the presence of humility (Luk18:14 — the tax collector who humbled himself goes home justified; 1Ki21:29 — Ahab's humbling delays judgment) reveals the image capable of right ordered response to God — the relational openness that constitutes the image-bearer as genuinely personal before God.

**[CHAR-2] Submission** (E)

The corpus evidences the absence of submission as indicating the image's deterioration in a person's relation to God. 2Th1:8 — "those who do not obey the gospel of our Lord Jesus" face divine judgment paired with "not knowing God" — the absence of submission is linked to fundamental relational rupture with God, not merely behavioural non-compliance. Rom6:16 — the will's habitual yielding to sin rather than righteousness discloses whose authority rules the inner person — the image is there but functioning in its inverted form, yielding to what destroys rather than what restores. Pro30:17 evidences the absence in the household register as a fatal moral failure. Conversely, the presence of submission — particularly obedience of faith (Rom1:5; Rom16:26) — marks the image functioning in right ordered relation to its Creator, with the person's will genuinely oriented toward God.

**[CHAR-3] Contrition** (E)

The presence of genuine contrition reveals an image-bearer who has maintained the capacity for moral self-knowledge before God — the person who can be genuinely broken in spirit by their own failure is one whose conscience and moral awareness are functioning genuinely. The absence of contrition before genuine moral failure (its mirror being hardness, denial, or self-justification) reveals an image in distortion — one that has closed itself to the moral reality of its own condition. Isa57:15's pairing of contrition with lowliness suggests that the characteristic is the acute expression of the same receptive orientation toward God that lowliness describes dispositionally; its absence marks the person who is constitutionally closed to God's reviving work.

**[CHAR-4] Meekness and gentleness** (E)

The presence of metriopatheō (Heb5:2) reveals an image-bearer who has appropriated their own creaturely limitation as the ground for compassionate engagement with others in their limitation — the characteristic requires a self-knowledge that translates into other-directedness. The absence of meekness/gentleness — severity or detachment toward the wayward — reveals an image-bearer who either has not genuinely reckoned with their own limitation (the false confidence of one who has not confronted their own weakness) or has reckoned with it without allowing it to generate compassionate response to others. Act9:38's readiness-without-hesitation (absence of okneō) reversed — hesitance, reluctance, self-protective delay — reveals an image-bearer who is turned inward rather than outward toward responsive service.

**[CHAR-5] Dignity** (E)

The presence of semnotēs in a person reveals an image-bearer who inhabits their role and existence with settled, grounded moral gravity — without the anxious self-assertion or self-promotional display that characterises the distorted image. 1Ti3:4 — the overseer "with all dignity keeping his children submissive" — the characteristic's presence enables ordered, non-coercive authority that reflects the image's proper ordering. The absence of semnotēs — replaced by either frivolousness (lack of seriousness) or proud self-display (the M08 register) — reveals an image in distortion: either insufficiently weighty or self-promotingly assertive rather than settled-and-grounded. The cross-register flag (M08 — structural opposite of proud self-display) is directly relevant here.

**[CHAR-6] Willing-heartedness** (E)

The presence of willing-heartedness reveals an image-bearer whose will is genuinely oriented toward God in free, uncoerced offering — the most authentic expression of the creature's responsive imaging of divine liberality. The VCG-01 evidence (Exo35:5/22; 1Ch28:21; 2Ch29:31) shows willing-hearted givers as those whose image-bearing is fully expressive in the offering-to-God register. Psa51:12 reveals by contrast: the loss of willing-heartedness through moral failure is a diminishment of the image's expressive capacity — the person who has lost the willing spirit cannot freely offer what the image calls for. The restoration sought in Psa51:12 is the restoration of the image's offering-capacity.

---

#### T0.4 — Typological Significance

##### T0.4.1 — Does Scripture use this characteristic typologically — deploying it to point toward or participate in a reality beyond the immediate (covenantal, eschatological, christological)?

**[CHAR-1] Humility** (E)

Yes. The OT ka.na evidence carries typological weight in two directions. First, the repeated pattern of Israel's kings humbling before God and receiving mercy (1Ki21:29; 2Ch12:7; 2Ch33:12; 2Ch34:27) functions as the narrative type of which Christ's self-humbling (Phili2:8) is the antitype — human figures humbling themselves before divine judgment prefigure the one who humbles himself definitively and without sin. Second, the tapeinōsis of Mary (Luk1:48 — "he has looked on the humble estate of his servant") carries typological Christological resonance: her lowly estate becomes the condition for the Incarnation, so that her tapeinōsis participates in the broader movement of God choosing the low and despised things (1Cor1:28 — outside this corpus but structurally related). Act8:33 (Christ's humiliation — "in his humiliation justice was denied him") explicitly interprets Isa53 as Christological fulfillment of the suffering-servant type.

**[CHAR-2] Submission** (E)

Yes, most significantly in the Christological direction. Rom5:19 deploys Christ's obedience typologically as the structural counter to Adam's disobedience — Adam's rebellion is the negative type of which Christ's submission is the redemptive antitype. Gen49:10 ("to him shall be the obedience of the peoples") functions typologically: the gathering of nations in submission to Judah's coming ruler is the OT type that Christological fulfillment realises in the submission of faith to Christ (Rom1:5; 16:26 — "obedience of faith among all the nations"). The gospel-obedience language explicitly connects to this typological arc: the obedience that was prophesied for the nations becomes the obedience of faith produced by the gospel.

**[CHAR-3] Contrition** (E)

The Isa57:15 verse carries typological potential. The high-and-holy God who dwells with the contrite anticipates the Incarnation — the divine taking up residence with those who are broken, which the NT reads as fulfilled in Christ's presence among the poor, the sinners, and the brokenhearted. Psa34:18 in the Psalms context is part of the wisdom-sufferer tradition that NT authors read Christologically (Psa34 is cited in 1Pe3:10–12 in its broader context). However, the direct typological deployment of contrition as a type pointing to a Christological reality is not explicitly evidenced within the two-verse corpus of §3.B alone. The typological resonances are present in the broader canonical context but not directly evidenced in these two verses.

**[CHAR-4] Meekness and gentleness** (E)

Yes, and this is the most significant typological dimension of the corpus. Heb5:2 belongs to the Hebrews argument in which the Aaronic high priest is a type pointing toward Christ as the superior high priest. The high priest's metriopatheō (gentleness grounded in shared weakness) is the type; Christ's sympathy with human weakness (Heb4:15 — outside corpus but the structural context) is the antitype. The human high priest's capacity for measured gentle dealing with the erring is a shadow of Christ's own compassionate mediation, which is deeper because his identification with human weakness is more thoroughgoing.

**[CHAR-5] Dignity** (S)

The three-verse corpus does not evidence typological use of semnotēs. The characteristic is addressed in paraenetic-communal contexts (public life, household leadership, teaching) without typological deployment pointing toward a Christological or eschatological reality beyond the immediate. The characteristic is addressed as a present community virtue rather than a typological indicator.

**[CHAR-6] Willing-heartedness** (E)

The Exodus tabernacle-building context (Exo35:5/22) carries typological resonance. The willing-hearted offering for the construction of the tabernacle (God's dwelling place among Israel) anticipates the NT community as the temple of the Spirit in which willing-hearted devotion is given. The pattern of free-will offering for God's dwelling place in the midst of his people is the OT type of the NT willing-hearted Spirit-dwelling in believers. Psa51:12's willing spirit as divine gift anticipates the NT giving of the Spirit who enables willing devotion (connected to the new covenant gift of the Spirit enabling genuine heartfelt obedience — Jer31:31–34 type/antitype, though outside corpus). The typological dimension is present in resonance rather than explicit deployment within §3.E alone.

---

##### T0.4.2 — If typological use is present, what is the direction of the typology — does the human instance point toward the divine, or does the divine instance establish the pattern for the human?

**[CHAR-1] Humility** (E)

The typology runs in both directions, and the evidence is explicit about this. In the Phili2 passage, the divine instance (Christ's self-humbling, v.8) explicitly establishes the pattern for the human (v.3 — "in humility count others more significant"), making the direction divine-to-human. However, in the OT ka.na narrative (1Ki21:29; 2Ch7:14; 2Ch33:12), the human instances of humbling before God point forward toward the one who will do so definitively — the human-to-divine direction. The dual directionality is itself significant: Christ's humbling is simultaneously the antitype of OT human humbling patterns and the prototype of NT Christian humility. The typological structure thus frames humility as the characteristic through which the divine and human meet — the Son condescends to take on the creaturely form of humility, and in doing so validates and redeems it as the primary posture of the creature before God.

**[CHAR-2] Submission** (E)

The typology runs in both directions. The Adam-Christ typology (Rom5:19) has the human negative instance (Adam's disobedience) pointing toward the divine positive instance (Christ's obedience) — human-to-divine direction. The Gen49:10 → Romans obedience-of-faith fulfilment runs the other direction: the OT eschatological promise (Gen49:10) is established as the divine-promissory pattern; the human response of faith-obedience (Rom1:5; 16:26) fulfils it — divine-to-human direction. Heb5:8 (Christ learning obedience through suffering) establishes the Christological paradigm that Paul picks up in Phili2:12 ("as you have always obeyed") — the divine instance (Christ's costly submission) establishes the pattern for the human community's sustained obedience.

**[CHAR-3] Contrition** (E)

To the extent typological resonance is present (T0.4.1), the direction appears to be human-to-divine: the contrite person's experience of God's drawing near (Psa34:18) and dwelling with them (Isa57:15) is a human instance that anticipates and points toward the fuller divine coming-to-the-broken in the Incarnation and the Spirit's indwelling of believers. The human instance of contrition opening to divine presence is a type of the fuller relational reality the gospel brings.

**[CHAR-4] Meekness and gentleness** (E)

The direction is *human-to-divine*: the human high priest's metriopatheō points toward Christ as the superior antitype — the human instance anticipates the divine-human fulfillment. The Aaronic high priest's gentleness (grounded in his own weakness) is the anticipatory type; Christ's priestly sympathy with human weakness (his full identification through the Incarnation) is the Christological antitype that the type points toward. The typological direction confirms the Christological Significance of the characteristic — it is not merely a human virtue but a mediating quality that points toward the divine-human mediator's character.

**[CHAR-5] Dignity** (S)

No typological use is evidenced in §3.D. Prompt closed.

**[CHAR-6] Willing-heartedness** (E)

The direction is *human-to-divine*: the human instances of willing-hearted offering for God's tabernacle (Exo35) and temple service (1Ch28:21; 2Ch29:31) are types pointing toward the fullness of willing Spirit-given devotion. The human pattern of willing-heartedness in the OT tabernacle/temple register points toward its NT Christological and pneumatological fulfillment. Psa51:12's prayer for a willing spirit points toward the Spirit-given willing orientation that the new covenant grants — the type (prayer for a willing spirit) anticipates the antitype (Spirit-given willing spirit).

---

##### T0.4.3 — If no typological use is evidenced, note this explicitly.

**[CHAR-1] Humility** (E)

Typological use is evidenced (see T0.4.1 and T0.4.2). This prompt is answered by those findings.

## T1 — Inner Being Faculty in Operation (24 prompts)

*Re-reading §3.E fresh for T1's lens.*

---

### T1

#### T1.1 — Name and Naming

##### T1.1.1 — What is this characteristic called in the programme — and what does the name itself signal about its essential nature?

**[CHAR-1] Humility** (E)

The characteristic is named Humility. The name signals a fundamentally relational orientation: to be humble is to be in a certain posture relative to God and to others — not a self-enclosed state but a positioned one. The name encompasses both the willed act of self-lowering (M09-A — the voluntary disposition) and the experienced state of being low (M09-B — the condition register), indicating that humility in Scripture names both what the person does and what the person finds themselves to be. The Hebrew terms tsa.na and tsa.nu.a (walking humbly, the humble) and ka.na (humbling, being humbled) carry relational direction built into their structure — humbling is always humbling *before* someone. The Greek tapeinos/tapeinofrosunē/tapeinoō family's root (tapeinos — low, base, not elevated) signals that the characteristic is defined by position: the person places themselves or finds themselves below their natural or claimed standing.

**[CHAR-2] Submission** (E)

The characteristic is named Submission. The name signals an essentially relational and directional structure: to submit is to place oneself under (sub-mittere) — to position the self in relation to a legitimate authority. The name implies both an agent (the one who submits) and a recipient (the authority submitted to), confirming that this is a constitutively relational characteristic that cannot operate in isolation. The name also implies an act: submission is done, not merely possessed — though as the evidence shows, it becomes a disposition through repetition and formation.

**[CHAR-3] Contrition** (E)

The characteristic is named Contrition. The name derives from the Latin conterere (to grind, crush, wear down) — signalling that this is the inner state of being ground down or broken, not merely softened. The name signals an acute, physically-resonant inner reality: the crushed spirit (Psa34:18 — "crushed in spirit"), the broken heart (Psa34:18 — "brokenhearted"), the contrite spirit (Isa57:15). The name thus immediately conveys the severity and intensity of the inner state — not mild regret but genuine interior breaking before moral reality.

**[CHAR-4] Meekness and gentleness** (E)

The characteristic is named Meekness and Gentleness. The name is compound, signalling that two related but distinct inner-being qualities are carried together: meekness (the inner capacity for governed strength — the will that does not deploy force inappropriately) and gentleness (the calibrated, compassionate quality of engagement with others in their weakness or need). The compound name reflects the two-term evidence corpus: metriopatheō (Heb5:2 — be gentle, deal measuredly with the erring) and okneō (Act9:38 — absence of hesitation, immediate volitional responsiveness). Neither term alone names both qualities; together they constitute the characteristic's dual nature.

**[CHAR-5] Dignity** (E)

The characteristic is named Dignity. The name signals a quality of inherent worth expressed in composed, grounded comportment — the person who has dignity carries themselves with settled moral seriousness that does not require external validation or self-promotion. The name points toward the inner-grounded nature of the quality: dignity is not conferred by status or performance but is the expression of settled inner worth. In the semnotēs corpus (1Ti2:2; 1Ti3:4; Tit2:7), the name signals a quality of the character that shapes how the person inhabits their role and relationships — weightily, seriously, without self-displaying performance.

**[CHAR-6] Willing-heartedness** (E)

The characteristic is named Willing-heartedness. The compound name signals both its constitutional location (the heart) and its constitutive quality (willing — freely-moved, uncoerced). The name points immediately toward the inner source of the characteristic: it is not willing-ness in general but willing-ness that originates in the heart as the integrating centre of the person. The name also signals the absence of compulsion as constitutive: willing-heartedness is specifically the quality of the heart that moves freely rather than under external pressure. Exo35:5 — "whoever is of a generous/willing heart" — and Psa51:12 — "a willing spirit" — together show the two constitutional locations (heart, spirit) that the compound name encompasses.

---

##### T1.1.2 — What do the primary Hebrew and Greek terms reveal at the definitional level — before deeper lexical analysis begins?

**[CHAR-1] Humility** (E)

At the definitional level, the Hebrew ka.na reveals humility as primarily an act: the verb form dominates the corpus, and almost every ka.na instance is the person *humbling* themselves or being humbled — a dynamic, event-like characteristic rather than a static trait. The Greek tapein- root reveals humility as primarily a disposition and quality: tapeinos (the adjective — "lowly in heart," Mat11:29) and tapeinofrosunē (the noun of quality — "with all humility," Eph4:2; Act20:19) name what the person *is*, while tapeinoō (the verb) names what the person *does* (Phili2:8; Jam4:10). Tapeinōsis names the *state* of being in lowliness (Luk1:48; Phili3:21). Together the vocabulary presents humility as simultaneously act (the willed event), disposition (the settled character), and condition (the state in which one finds oneself) — three registers that the volume-split between M09-A and M09-B captures structurally.

**[CHAR-2] Submission** (E)

Primary terms: *H3349 yiq.qe.hah* — the Hebrew term in Gen49:10 meaning obedience, yielding of allegiance. *G5218 hupakoē* — noun from hupo (under) + akouō (to hear) — literally "under-hearing," the attentive receptivity that hears and yields to what is heard. *G5219 hupakouō* — the verbal form: to hear under, to obey. *G5292 hupotagē* — noun from hupo (under) + tassō (to arrange, order) — being ordered under; the condition of being placed in ordered submission. The roots reveal that submission in both its Greek forms is constitutively auditory-relational (hupakoē — hearing-under) and structural-relational (hupotagē — ordered under). Both imply a legitimate structure within which the yielding occurs — not arbitrary capitulation but ordered responsiveness within a right relationship.

**[CHAR-3] Contrition** (E)

The sole term is H1793A dak.ka — an adjective/noun meaning crushed, pulverised, reduced to dust. The root dkk conveys thoroughgoing reduction to a lower state — not merely bent or lowered but ground down. In Psa34:18 it modifies the spirit ("the crushed/dak.ka in spirit"); in Isa57:15 it describes the "contrite and lowly spirit" alongside anaw/lowly. At the definitional level, the term reveals that contrition is the most acute and thoroughgoing form of interior diminishment — not a mild self-assessment but a crushing of the spirit's self-sufficiency and pride. The term's root in grinding/pulverising also suggests the process character of the state — it is the result of a crushing experience rather than a chosen posture.

**[CHAR-4] Meekness and gentleness** (E)

*G3356 metriopatheō* (Heb5:2): compound of metrios (measured, moderate, within measure) + patheō (to experience, suffer, feel passion). The etymological sense is "to feel/experience within measure" — calibrated emotional engagement that does not go to excess in either direction (neither harsh severity nor indulgent permissiveness). *G3635 okneō* (Act9:38): from oknos (hesitation, reluctance, sloth); used here in the negative — the disciples urge Peter to come "without okneō" — naming the absence of hesitation. At the definitional level: metriopatheō defines the *quality of engagement* (measured, compassionate); okneō defines the *quality of readiness* (unimpeded, non-hesitating). Together they define the characteristic as measured-engaged-and-ready — not passive but actively calibrated.

**[CHAR-5] Dignity** (E)

G4587 semnotēs — noun from semnos (venerable, worthy of reverence, august, serious). The root connects to sebomai (to reverence, to worship) — the same family as the godliness vocabulary. The term names a quality of serious, weighty character that naturally commands respect rather than demanding it: the person of semnotēs does not need to assert their authority or worth because the settled gravity of their character does this without performance. At the definitional level, the term reveals dignity as *reverence-worthiness from the inside* — the quality that makes a person naturally worthy of reverent regard through their settled moral seriousness rather than through claimed status.

**[CHAR-6] Willing-heartedness** (E)

H5081G na.div — adjective/noun from a root related to nadab (to give voluntarily, to offer freely). Na.div names one who is noble-hearted, generous, willing — specifically in the sense of freely-given inner readiness. The root nadab carries the sense of the will that springs forward freely rather than being pushed from behind. At the definitional level, the term reveals willing-heartedness as the inner posture of the will that is self-initiated and uncoerced — the will that moves toward giving and service from within rather than from external compulsion. The na.div is thus the freely-springing inner movement of the will toward God and others.

---

##### T1.1.3 — Does the name carry directional, relational, or constitutional implications that orient the enquiry from the outset?

**[CHAR-1] Humility** (E)

Yes, all three. *Directional:* humility is consistently downward — toward God (Jam4:10; 1Pe5:6; 2Ch7:14), toward others (Phili2:3; Eph4:2; 1Pe5:5), and away from self-elevation (Mat23:12; Luk14:11). The direction is built into the name. *Relational:* ka.na and the Greek tapein- terms are almost never reflexive-only; they name a posture before another (before God, before elders, before one another). The relational target is structural to the characteristic, not incidental. *Constitutional:* Mat11:29 locates lowliness explicitly "in the heart" — the innermost personal centre. Lev26:41 names the "uncircumcised heart" that must be broken for humility to emerge. The characteristic is constitutionally interior: the relational posture originates from the heart's orientation, not from external behaviour alone.

**[CHAR-2] Submission** (E)

All three. *Directional:* submission runs downward in the authority-structure sense — toward God (Rom1:5; Heb5:9), toward parents (Eph6:1; Col3:20), toward legitimate apostolic instruction (Phili2:12; 2Th3:14). *Relational:* the terms are constitutively relational — one submits to someone; the characteristic is always triangulated between the submitting person, the authority, and the ground of the submission (theological — "in the Lord," Eph6:1; "as to Christ," Eph6:5). *Constitutional:* Rom6:17 ("obedient from the heart") and Phili2:12 (sustained without external accountability — in "my absence") locate the characteristic constitutionally in the heart and conscience rather than in external compliance — it must originate in the inner person to be genuine.

**[CHAR-3] Contrition** (E)

All three. *Constitutional:* Psa34:18 locates the characteristic in spirit ("crushed in spirit") and heart ("brokenhearted") — two explicit constitutional locations. *Relational:* both verses are constitutively Godward — contrition is the inner state before God specifically; Isa57:15 names God's dwelling with the contrite as the relational orientation. The characteristic does not exist in isolation but always in relation to the divine holy presence that occasions or responds to it. *Directional:* the crushing is downward — from self-sufficiency and pride toward genuine interior brokenness. The direction is the opposite of self-assertion; the crushed spirit is the one that no longer stands in self-sufficiency before God.

**[CHAR-4] Meekness and gentleness** (E)

All three. *Relational:* both terms are constitutively relational — metriopatheō names the quality of the high priest's engagement *with* the erring (Heb5:2 — "deal gently with the ignorant and wayward"); okneō names the responsiveness *to* others' call for help (Act9:38 — "come to us without delay"). The characteristic is always directed toward another. *Constitutional:* metriopatheō grounds the capacity in "shared weakness" (Heb5:2) — an inner awareness of one's own creaturely limitation that mediates the character of engagement. *Directional:* the characteristic's movement is outward toward others in need — the meek/gentle person moves toward the erring and the calling without self-protective hesitation.

**[CHAR-5] Dignity** (E)

All three. *Relational:* semnotēs is always visible in relation — 1Ti2:2 in public life, 1Ti3:4 in household governance, Tit2:7 in teaching. The characteristic shapes how the person inhabits their social relationships and roles. *Constitutional:* the characteristic is named as an inner quality (Pass A meanings: "inner gravity," "sober weighty seriousness of character," "inner quality") that expresses itself outwardly. *Directional:* the characteristic's direction is inward-first (settled inner moral gravity) expressing outward (in public life, household, teaching) — from the grounded inner person outward into social existence, not from social expectation inward.

**[CHAR-6] Willing-heartedness** (E)

All three. *Directional:* the freely-moved heart/spirit moves *toward* God and toward his service — Exo35:5 (toward the Lord's contribution), 2Ch29:31 (toward burnt offerings), Psa51:12 (toward joyful devotion). The direction is outward-Godward from the inner person. *Relational:* the characteristic is constitutively relational toward God — the free offering is always directed toward the one who calls for it (Exo35:5 — "a contribution to the Lord"). *Constitutional:* the characteristic is located in the heart (Exo35:5 — "of a generous/willing heart"; Exo35:22; 2Ch29:31 — "willing heart") and in the spirit (Psa51:12 — "a willing spirit"). Both the heart and spirit are named as constitutional locations.

---

#### T1.2 — Kind

##### T1.2.1 — What kind of inner-being phenomenon does this characteristic appear to be — an act, a disposition, a condition, a quality, or something else?

**[CHAR-1] Humility** (E)

The corpus evidences all four. As *act*: the ka.na verbal corpus presents humbling as a single datable event — Manasseh humbled himself greatly in distress (2Ch33:12), Josiah humbled himself when he heard God's words (2Ch34:27), Jam4:10 commands "humble yourselves before the Lord." As *disposition*: tapeinofrosunē in Act20:19 names Paul's pervading manner of ministry; in Eph4:2 and Col3:12 it is a character-garment worn continuously. As *condition*: tapeinōsis in Luk1:48 names Mary's estate; Phili3:21 names the lowly body as the present creaturely condition. As *quality*: Mat11:29 names lowliness in the heart as a character quality Jesus invites others to learn. The characteristic is thus genuinely multi-modal — it operates as an event, a character-disposition, a condition one inhabits, and a quality of inner being, with the corpus distributing examples across all four.

**[CHAR-2] Submission** (E)

The corpus evidences submission simultaneously as act, disposition, condition, and quality. As *act*: Gal2:5 ("we did not yield in submission even for a moment" — the volitional act that can be withheld); Heb11:8 (Abraham obeyed the call — a decisive volitional event). As *disposition*: Phili2:12 ("as you have always obeyed" — sustained inner orientation); 1Pe1:14 ("as obedient children" — the characteristic as a defining personal identity). As *condition*: 1Pe1:2 (obedience as the goal-state the Spirit's sanctifying work directs toward). As *quality*: Rom16:19 (obedience publicly known as a characteristic of the community's will). The M09-C/D split maps onto this: M09-C is primarily the act-and-disposition register; M09-D is primarily the quality-and-condition register of obedience as a known characteristic of persons and communities.

**[CHAR-3] Contrition** (E)

The corpus evidences contrition primarily as a *condition and state* rather than an act or disposition. The passive/resultant quality of dak.ka (crushed — the result of a grinding process) frames it as something the person finds themselves in rather than something they choose or generate. Psa34:18 ("brokenhearted," "crushed in spirit") names states of being. Isa57:15 ("who is of a contrite and lowly spirit") names a characterological state. However, since genuine contrition requires the person's genuine moral self-knowledge and openness to it, there is a volitional element: the person who genuinely confronts their failure rather than defending against it participates volitionally in the state's formation. The characteristic is primarily a *condition that contains volitional elements* — the person's willingness to remain in the crushed state rather than flee from it.

**[CHAR-4] Meekness and gentleness** (E)

Both terms evidence the characteristic primarily as a *capacity and quality* rather than as a momentary act or a passive condition. Metriopatheō (Heb5:2) — "he can deal gently" — is a capacity (can = has the capacity for). Okneō as named absence (Act9:38) — the term names the *quality of responsiveness* as what is expected and what should not be lacking. The characteristic is thus the *inner capacity for calibrated, unimpeded engagement with others* — a quality the person has or lacks rather than an action they perform on a specific occasion. The quality is dispositional: it characterises the person's manner of engaging rather than a single event.

**[CHAR-5] Dignity** (E)

The corpus evidences semnotēs primarily as a *stable character quality*: 1Ti2:2 — the inner quality characterising a peaceful life; 1Ti3:4 — the character quality from which household governance flows; Tit2:7 — a quality of teaching character. All three use semnotēs as a quality or character-disposition rather than as a momentary act or acute state. The characteristic is the person's stable inner gravitas that consistently shapes their manner of inhabiting their life and roles — a characterological quality rather than an event or condition.

**[CHAR-6] Willing-heartedness** (E)

The corpus evidences willing-heartedness primarily as a *disposition and quality* of the heart and spirit. Exo35:5 — "whoever is of a generous/willing heart" — naming the person by the disposition of their heart; Exo35:22 — "all who were of a willing heart" — the characterological description; 1Ch28:21 — "every willing man" — person-type vocabulary. Psa51:12 — "a willing spirit" — the spirit as a characterological disposition. The characteristic is the settled inner orientation of the will rather than a momentary act — it is what the heart and spirit are, not merely what they do at a given moment. However, it also produces acts (bringing offerings) — so it is a disposition that issues in acts.

---

##### T1.2.2 — Is this characteristic simple in structure or does it appear to have constituent elements at first encounter?

**[CHAR-1] Humility** (E)

Humility has constituent elements visible at first encounter. The volume-split between M09-A (willed self-lowering) and M09-B (experienced/imposed lowliness) represents the primary structural distinction: the voluntary-dispositional register and the condition-state register are genuinely different in structure even when they name the same faculty. Within M09-A, further constituent elements appear: (a) the cognitive dimension — Phili2:3 defines humility as *counting others more significant* (a volitional cognitive act); (b) the affective dimension — 2Ki22:19 pairs humbling with weeping as its expression; (c) the volitional dimension — tapeinoō throughout is the deliberate act of the will bowing down. The Colossian material (Col2:18; Col2:23) adds a fourth: the authenticity dimension — the distinction between performed humility and genuine inner transformation. The characteristic is thus structured rather than simple, with cognitive, affective, volitional, and authenticity constituents.

**[CHAR-2] Submission** (E)

The characteristic has constituent elements visible at first encounter. The volume-split between M09-C (heart/conscience-located inner disposition) and M09-D (relational pattern of obedience-in-context) is itself a structural distinction: inner disposition versus lived expression. Within M09-C, constituent elements include: (a) the allegiance dimension — the will's positioning toward an authority (Rom6:16/17); (b) the cognitive-volitional dimension — taking every thought captive (2Cor10:5); (c) the sincerity/motive dimension — "with sincere heart" versus "eye-service" (Eph6:5; Col3:22); (d) the learned-and-costly dimension — obedience acquired through suffering (Heb5:8). These are genuinely distinct: a person might have behavioural compliance (M09-D register) without the heart-located genuineness (M09-C), and the Colossian and Ephesian material explicitly distinguishes these.

**[CHAR-3] Contrition** (E)

Within the two-verse corpus, the characteristic appears to have two constituent elements. First, the *brokenness/crushing element*: the spirit crushed, the heart broken — the interior collapse of self-sufficiency (Psa34:18). Second, the *lowliness element*: Isa57:15 pairs contrition with lowliness ("contrite and lowly in spirit") — the crushed state has a lowliness dimension that relates it to CHAR-1 Humility. The two elements are distinguishable: brokenness is the acute experiential condition; lowliness is the resulting relational posture before God. Together they constitute contrition as an acute crushing that produces a lowered relational posture before God.

**[CHAR-4] Meekness and gentleness** (E)

The evidence evidences two constituent elements. First, the *calibrated-restraint element* (metriopatheō — Heb5:2): the capacity to hold force, judgment, or severity in measured response — not to go to excess in either direction. Second, the *volitional-readiness element* (okneō — Act9:38): the absence of resistant hesitation — the inner freedom that does not create friction between perceiving a genuine need and responding to it. These are distinguishable: one can have calibrated gentleness without instant responsiveness (the gentle person who nevertheless delays); or volitional readiness without calibrated gentleness (the person who responds immediately but without measured compassion). Together they define the full characteristic.

**[CHAR-5] Dignity** (E)

Within the three-verse corpus, the characteristic appears to have two constituent elements evidenced across the three contexts. First, *settled moral gravity* — the inner seriousness and weightiness of character (1Ti2:2 — "sober, weighty seriousness"; Tit2:7 — "gravitas and moral seriousness"). Second, *non-self-promoting self-possession* — the quality does not require display or assertion; it operates from a secure inner ground (1Ti3:4 — the character quality "from which household governance flows" rather than from which power is asserted). The two elements together: settled seriousness and grounded self-possession that does not need performance.

**[CHAR-6] Willing-heartedness** (E)
