The two VCGs evidence two constituent registers. VCG-01 (Exo35:5/22; 1Ch28:21; 2Ch29:31): *freely-moved generosity* — the willing heart as the inner source of spontaneous, uncoerced giving and service toward God; the positive, active, outward-giving dimension. VCG-02 (Psa51:12): *divine-gift willing spirit* — willingness as something that can be lost and must be divinely restored; the vulnerability and restoration dimension. Together the two registers constitute the full characteristic: freely-given inner readiness (VCG-01) and the same readiness as divine gift that requires sustaining (VCG-02). The constituent elements are: spontaneous self-generated willingness (VCG-01) and received/restored willingness (VCG-02).

---

##### T1.2.3 — What is the current best working description of this characteristic — encapsulating its constitutional location, the faculties it primarily engages, and its impact on the person?

**[CHAR-1] Humility** (E)

The current best working description, drawn from the evidence corpus: *Humility is the inner disposition in which the person voluntarily relinquishes the claim to their natural or assumed standing — before God, in response to his word and authority, and before others, in recognition of their worth — while also naming the state in which the person finds themselves lowered by circumstance, affliction, or divine action. It is constitutionally located in the heart (Mat11:29; Lev26:41), engages the will (Jam4:10; Phili2:8), cognitive evaluation (Phili2:3), and affect (2Ki22:19; 2Ch34:27), and is characterised by an openness to God and others that self-assertion closes.* The M09-A register (willed self-lowering) represents the primary characteristic; the M09-B register (experienced lowliness) represents the same faculty operating under external pressure or as the state in which the person awaits God's reversal.

**[CHAR-2] Submission** (E)

The current best working description, drawn from the evidence: *Submission is the inner disposition of willing yielding of the will to a legitimate authority — God, apostolic instruction, household structures, or the gospel — originating in the heart and conscience rather than from external pressure, expressed in the relational pattern of the person's life across varying contexts. It operates both as a decisive volitional act (Gal2:5; Heb11:8) and as a sustained character-orientation (Phili2:12; 1Pe1:14). In its deepest form it is not compliance but inner conformity — the will's genuine alignment with the authority's claim, rooted in trust, conscience, and (in the NT register) faith.* The M09-C register names the inner-disposition face; the M09-D register names the relational-pattern face of the same characteristic.

**[CHAR-3] Contrition** (E)

The current best working description from the evidence: *Contrition is the acute, grief-laden inner state of being crushed and broken in spirit — the interior collapse that follows genuine confrontation with one's own moral failure or with the holiness of God. It is located in spirit and heart (Psa34:18; Isa57:15), paired with lowliness as its relational posture (Isa57:15), and is constitutively oriented toward God who responds to it with nearness, saving action, and reviving presence. It is the most intensive register of interior self-knowledge before God — not mild regret but genuine crushing of the inner person's self-sufficiency before divine holiness.*

**[CHAR-4] Meekness and gentleness** (E)

The current best working description from the evidence: *Meekness and Gentleness is the inner capacity for calibrated, compassionate engagement with others in their weakness, waywardness, or need — holding strength, judgment, or force within measured restraint (metriopatheō) grounded in genuine awareness of one's own creaturely limitation (Heb5:2), combined with the volitional readiness that does not hesitate or resist when called to respond (okneō, Act9:38). The characteristic is not passivity but governed strength — the capacity to hold what one has without deploying it inappropriately, and to respond without self-protective delay.*

**[CHAR-5] Dignity** (E)

The current best working description from the evidence: *Dignity (semnotēs) is the settled inner quality of moral gravity and seriousness of character — the inner gravitas of the person who inhabits their life, roles, and relationships with composed, weighty seriousness rather than with self-promoting performance or frivolous self-display. It is constitutionally an inner quality (1Ti2:2; 1Ti3:4; Tit2:7) that consistently shapes the character and manner of the person's public existence, household leadership, and teaching — authenticating and ordering these from the inside rather than requiring external support for their authority.*

**[CHAR-6] Willing-heartedness** (E)

The current best working description from the evidence: *Willing-heartedness (na.div) is the inner disposition of the heart and spirit in which the will moves freely and gladly toward giving and service to God — uncoerced, spontaneously motivated from within (Exo35:5/22; 1Ch28:21; 2Ch29:31). This freely-moved inner readiness is the source of genuine offering and wholehearted service rather than compelled contribution. It is also a disposition that can be lost through moral failure and requires divine sustaining to persist (Psa51:12 — "uphold me with a willing spirit"), positioning it as both a natural inner posture of the genuinely committed person and a gift of God to be sought and maintained.*

---

#### T1.3 — Boundary

##### T1.3.1 — What is the structural opposite of this characteristic — the inner-being reality that stands against or excludes it?

**[CHAR-1] Humility** (E)

The corpus names the structural opposite explicitly and repeatedly: pride (Hebrew ga.on / Greek hyperēphanos). Pro11:2 sets the direct antithesis: "when pride comes, then comes disgrace; with the humble is wisdom." Jam4:6 and 1Pe5:5 both cite the same formula: "God opposes the proud but gives grace to the humble" — structurally pairing the proud and the humble as the two positions before God. Mat23:12 and its parallels (Luk14:11; Luk18:14) oppose the one who "exalts himself" against the one who "humbles himself." 2Ch32:26 identifies Hezekiah's humbling as explicitly "for the pride of his heart" — pride is the inner condition humility addresses and replaces. The structural opposite is pride as the inner posture of self-assertion, self-elevation, and refusal to acknowledge one's true standing before God and others.

**[CHAR-2] Submission** (E)

The corpus names the structural opposite as disobedience and inner refusal. Rom5:19 — "the one man's disobedience" (Adam) as the structural counter to "the one man's obedience" (Christ). 2Th1:8 — "those who do not obey the gospel" as the structural opposite of the obedient. Pro30:17 — the mocking eye that "scorns to obey" a mother. 2Th3:14 — the one who "does not obey" Paul's letter. The structural opposite is not merely external non-compliance but the inner refusal of the will to yield — the self that asserts its own authority over against the legitimate claim. Rom6:16 names this most precisely: the will's habitual yielding to sin rather than to righteousness is the structural opposite, and it determines whose servant one is.

**[CHAR-3] Contrition** (E)

The corpus does not name the structural opposite directly, but the contrast structure of Isa57:15 implies it: God dwells with "the contrite and lowly spirit" — implying he does not dwell in the same way with the non-contrite, self-sufficient, proud spirit. The structural opposite is the *hardened, proud, or self-sufficient spirit* that does not register or respond to its own moral condition before God. The dak.ka root (crushed) contrasts structurally with the hard or elevated — the spirit that has not been brought down or crushed by genuine moral confrontation.

**[CHAR-4] Meekness and gentleness** (E)

The corpus implies two structural opposites corresponding to the two elements. The structural opposite of metriopatheō (calibrated gentle engagement) is *severity or harsh judgment* toward the erring — the high priest who "cannot deal gently" with waywardness would be one who responds with disproportionate severity or cold detachment rather than measured compassion. The structural opposite of the okneō absence (unimpeded readiness) is *hesitation, reluctance, and sloth* (oknos — the root of okneō) — the person whose will resists or delays in responding to genuine need. Together: severity/detachment and hesitant reluctance are the structural opposites.

**[CHAR-5] Dignity** (E)

The structural opposite is evidenced through the carry-forward cross-register flag: M08 (Pride/Arrogance/Boasting) represents proud self-display — the anxious assertion of worth through performance and status-seeking. Semnotēs is the inner-grounded settled gravity that does not need to assert; M08's proud self-display is the self-asserting performance that reveals the absence of genuine inner-grounded worth. Additionally, the structural opposite could include *frivolousness or lightness* — the lack of serious moral gravity that fails to authenticate teaching or leadership (Tit2:7 — dignity as authenticating quality implies its absence would undermine authentication). The primary structural opposite is M08-register proud self-display; the secondary is moral frivolousness.

**[CHAR-6] Willing-heartedness** (E)

The structural opposite is implied throughout the corpus as *reluctance, compulsion, and unwilling compliance* — the giving that happens under external pressure or obligation rather than from internal free movement. Exo35:5 — "whoever is of a generous/willing heart let him bring" — implies that those whose hearts are not willing do not bring, or bring under compulsion. Psa51:12's request for a willing spirit implies the opposite state: the spirit that is not willing — dragged, heavy, unable to freely offer. Additionally, the structural opposite connects to M08 (Pride/self-assertion) and to the broader resistant will that self-protects rather than freely gives. Psa51:12's context of moral failure suggests the opposite state is the spirit burdened by guilt that cannot freely give.

---

##### T1.3.2 — What does this characteristic explicitly exclude or resist?

**[CHAR-1] Humility** (E)

The corpus reveals several explicit exclusions. (a) *Self-exaltation:* Mat23:12 — the self-exalting and the self-humbling are direct opposites; the humble do not assert their standing. (b) *Conceit and selfish ambition:* Phili2:3 — humility is defined as the direct counter to conceit (kenodoxia) and selfish ambition (eritheia). (c) *Performed or externally-driven self-lowering:* Col2:18 and Col2:23 expose humility that is outward performance masking inner pride — the characteristic explicitly excludes pseudo-humility that is self-focused. (d) *Haughtiness and social hierarchy:* Rom12:16 — associating with the lowly is contrasted with being "haughty" and being "wise in your own sight." (e) *The refusal to hear God's word:* 2Ch36:12 — Zedekiah's refusal to humble himself before the prophet is his failure to receive divine address; humility includes openness to being spoken to and corrected by God.

**[CHAR-2] Submission** (E)

The corpus reveals several explicit exclusions. (a) *Eye-service and people-pleasing:* Eph6:5 and Col3:22 both explicitly contrast sincere-heart submission with "eye-service" and "people-pleasing" — the characteristic excludes compliance driven by social observation or approval. (b) *Yielding to false authority:* Gal2:5 — Paul's non-submission to false teachers is the principled exercise of the same capacity. The characteristic excludes submission to illegitimate or gospel-distorting authority. (c) *Mere ignorance-compliance:* 2Th1:8 frames disobedience as paired with "not knowing God" — genuine submission is not identical with mere ignorance-compliance or cultural conformity; it involves inner knowledge and orientation. (d) *Inner refusal:* 2Th3:14 frames the one who does not obey as having an "inner refusal" that the communal response must address.

**[CHAR-3] Contrition** (E)

The characteristic excludes *self-sufficiency and pride* as inner orientations: the crushed spirit (Psa34:18) is by definition one that no longer stands in proud self-sufficiency. It excludes *self-justification* — genuine contrition requires honest confrontation with moral failure rather than denial or defence. It excludes *detachment from one's own moral condition* — the broken heart (Psa34:18) is one that genuinely registers the weight of what has occurred rather than remaining analytically distant from it. The characteristic is incompatible with the self-protective deflection of genuine moral reality.

**[CHAR-4] Meekness and gentleness** (E)

Metriopatheō explicitly excludes *excessive severity* (the harsh judgment that exceeds what is appropriate toward the ignorant and wayward — Heb5:2) and *cold detachment* (the priestly withdrawal that does not engage with the erring's condition). The grounding in "shared weakness" (Heb5:2) also excludes *prideful superiority* — the gentle dealing is incompatible with the stance of the one who has not acknowledged their own weakness. Okneō's negation (Act9:38 — "without delay") explicitly excludes *hesitation and reluctance* — the inward dragging of the will that delays genuine responsiveness. The characteristic excludes self-protective inwardness in both its engagement-quality (severity/detachment) and its responsiveness-quality (hesitation/delay).

**[CHAR-5] Dignity** (E)

The three-verse corpus evidences the following exclusions. (a) *Self-promotion and performance:* the inner-grounded quality of semnotēs excludes the need to perform, assert, or display — 1Ti3:4's dignity producing household order from the inside rather than through imposed force implies the characteristic is incompatible with leadership-by-self-assertion. (b) *Frivolousness and lack of moral seriousness:* Tit2:7 — dignity alongside integrity authenticating teaching — implies the characteristic excludes levity or moral flippancy in contexts requiring seriousness. (c) *Unstable self-presentation:* 1Ti2:2's dignity enabling a "peaceful and quiet life" implies the characteristic excludes the anxious, unstable self-presentation that requires constant external validation.

**[CHAR-6] Willing-heartedness** (E)

Exo35:5 — "whoever is of a willing heart" — explicitly structures the offering around the inner condition rather than external compulsion: the characteristic excludes giving that originates in external pressure, social obligation, or duty-compliance rather than inner free movement. 1Ch28:21 — "every willing man who has skill" — the willingness is paired with skill but distinguished from compulsion: the characteristic excludes skilled service done under force or fear. Psa51:12's request for upholding implies the characteristic excludes the self-generated will that has collapsed — it is not mere willpower but the freely-springing inner readiness that can only be divinely sustained after failure.

---

##### T1.3.3 — What is this characteristic not — where does it end and something else begin?

**[CHAR-1] Humility** (E)

The Colossian material makes the clearest boundary statement: Col2:18 and Col2:23 distinguish humility from *performed asceticism and self-denial* — outward severity to the body that has the *appearance* of wisdom and humility but cannot touch the inner disposition. Humility is not the same as self-abasement for its own sake or as social display. Additionally, the volume-split itself defines a boundary: humility (CHAR-1) is distinct from *contrition* (CHAR-3 in M09 — dak.ka), which is the acute, grief-laden form specifically following moral failure. Contrition is the crisis-precipitated acute form; humility is the stable disposition and its experiential states. A further boundary: humility is not the same as *submission to authority* (CHAR-2 — the M09-C/D register), though the two overlap and interact. Submission is the will's yielding to a specific authority; humility is the inner posture of the whole person before God and others generally.

**[CHAR-2] Submission** (E)

The evidence establishes two key boundaries. First, submission is not identical with *humility* (CHAR-1), though the two are closely related. Humility is the inner posture of self-lowering before God and others generally; submission is the will's yielding to a specific legitimate authority. Phili2:8 shows them working together — Christ "humbled himself by becoming obedient" — but the difference is that one can be genuinely humble without being in an act of specific authority-submission. Second, submission is not mere *compliance* without inner conviction. The entire M09-C corpus (heart-located inner disposition) distinguishes genuine submission from behavioural compliance — Phili2:12 (persistence without external accountability), Col3:22 (from sincerity of heart, not eye-service), 1Pe1:22 (obedience to truth that purifies the soul). Where compliance ends, submission — in its M09 register — begins.

**[CHAR-3] Contrition** (E)

Two boundary distinctions are supported by the evidence. First, contrition is not the same as *humility* (CHAR-1) — Isa57:15 explicitly pairs them as distinct ("contrite and lowly in spirit") — they co-occur but are distinguishable. Humility is the stable dispositional posture; contrition is the acute experiential state specifically occasioned by moral failure or divine-holiness encounter. Second, contrition is not *despair* — both verses evidence God's responsive nearness and reviving as the outcome (Psa34:18 — saves; Isa57:15 — revives). The characteristic is acute inner breaking that opens toward God's restoration, not closing-down despair that excludes God. Where the opening toward God's reviving is absent, the inner state is not contrition but something else (despair, shame, or hardening).

**[CHAR-4] Meekness and gentleness** (E)

Two boundaries are supported. First, meekness/gentleness is not the same as *permissiveness or indulgence*: metriopatheō (Heb5:2) is measured — "within measure" — implying that the gentleness does not abandon all correction or accountability, merely calibrates it appropriately. The high priest who "deals gently with the ignorant" still deals with them; the gentleness is in the manner rather than the absence of engagement. Second, okneō (Act9:38) names unimpeded responsiveness specifically in the context of answering a call from others — it is not the same as reckless impulsivity (which would be absence of discernment) but the absence of self-protective reluctance. The characteristic ends where either permissive-boundary-dissolution begins (against metriopatheō's measured character) or where impulsive undiscerned reaction begins (against okneō's grounded responsiveness).

**[CHAR-5] Dignity** (E)

Two boundaries are evidenced. First, semnotēs is not *social honour-rank* — the Pass A meanings explicitly note "not the social-honour-rank sense." The characteristic is an inner quality of character rather than a conferred social status. A person can have social honour without semnotēs (empty dignity) or semnotēs without high social position (the person of settled moral gravity in any social location). Second, semnotēs is not *severity or harshness* — the characteristic is grounded gravity, not aggressive weight. 1Ti3:4 — the overseer's dignity producing household order — implies the order emerges from the character quality rather than from coercive force; the dignity is not identical to rigidity or harshness.

**[CHAR-6] Willing-heartedness** (E)

Three boundaries are supported. First, willing-heartedness is not *mere willpower or determination* — Psa51:12 ("uphold me with a willing spirit") evidences that genuine willingness cannot be sustained by sheer self-determination after moral failure; it requires divine upholding. The characteristic ends where it becomes self-reliant willpower. Second, willing-heartedness is not the same as *submission (CHAR-2)* — submission is the will's yielding to a specific authority; willing-heartedness is the will's spontaneous movement toward giving and service. The difference: submission is responsive to a command; willing-heartedness is self-initiated from inner motivation. Third, willing-heartedness is not *compelled compliance* — the corpus consistently names it as the non-compelled, freely-generated inner movement (Exo35:5/22 — not commanded contributions but voluntary).

---

#### T1.4 — Modes of Operation

##### T1.4.1 — In what distinct ways does this characteristic operate within the inner person?

**[CHAR-1] Humility** (E)

The corpus evidences at least four distinct modes of inner operation. (a) *As an initiating act of the will:* humbling oneself (tapeinoō in Jam4:10; 1Pe5:6; 2Ch7:14) is a deliberate volitional event that initiates a posture before God. (b) *As a pervading disposition of character:* tapeinofrosunē in Act20:19 pervades Paul's whole manner of ministry; in Eph4:2 it characterises every relational interaction — the characteristic colours the whole inner person rather than emerging only on occasion. (c) *As a cognitive reordering:* Phili2:3 — humility is the act of *counting others more significant than oneself* — a deliberate cognitive re-evaluation of relative worth. (d) *As an inner response to divine address:* in the ka.na corpus, humbling is the inner movement of the heart when confronted with God's word, judgment, or claim (2Ch34:27; 2Ch36:12 negative instance). The four modes are distinguishable: volitional, dispositional, cognitive, and responsive.

**[CHAR-2] Submission** (E)

Four distinct modes are evidenced. (a) *As volitional allegiance-positioning:* Rom6:16/17 — the will's habitual orienting toward an authority that constitutes whose servant one is; a continuous inner disposition of the will. (b) *As cognitive-volitional capture:* 2Cor10:5 — taking every thought captive to obey Christ; the characteristic engages the mind's reasoning and captures it for Christ's authority. (c) *As sincere-motive genuineness:* Eph6:5; Phili2:12; Col3:22 — the submission operates at the level of genuine inner motive (sincere heart, fear of God) rather than behavioural surface. (d) *As learned-costly-transformative process:* Heb5:8 — Christ learned obedience through suffering; 1Pe1:22 — obedience purifies the soul; the characteristic operates as a formative process that shapes the inner person over time.

**[CHAR-3] Contrition** (E)

Two distinct modes are evidenced. First, as *acute experiential breaking*: the spirit is crushed, the heart is broken (Psa34:18) — the characteristic operates as an interior collapse that the person undergoes rather than controls. Second, as *an inner posture that opens toward God*: Isa57:15 — God "dwells with" the contrite — the characteristic operates as the inner state that makes divine indwelling possible; it is the condition that does not resist God's reviving presence but opens to receive it. The first mode is the experiential (what happens to the person); the second is the relational-receptive (what the state enables in the person's relation to God).

**[CHAR-4] Meekness and gentleness** (E)

Two distinct modes are evidenced. First, *as calibrated capacity for engagement*: metriopatheō (Heb5:2) — the inner quality that modulates one's manner of dealing with others in their weakness; the characteristic operates as a governing inner disposition that shapes the quality and tone of engagement. Second, *as volitional readiness without friction*: okneō (Act9:38) — the characteristic operates as the absence of an inner blocking force; the will that does not generate the hesitation-friction that delays responsiveness. The first mode is qualitative (how one engages); the second is kinetic (whether the will moves promptly or resists).

**[CHAR-5] Dignity** (E)

Two modes are evidenced. First, *as a shaping character quality*: the characteristic consistently shapes how the person inhabits their life and roles (1Ti2:2 — shapes public life; 1Ti3:4 — shapes household governance; Tit2:7 — shapes teaching manner). The characteristic is not an episodic quality but a continuous shaper of the person's manner of existing. Second, *as an authenticating inner ground*: Tit2:7 — dignity alongside integrity "authenticates instruction" — the characteristic operates as the inner ground that makes the person's external activity genuine rather than performative. Without the inner semnotēs, the teaching would lack authentic weight regardless of its content.

**[CHAR-6] Willing-heartedness** (E)

Two distinct modes are evidenced corresponding to the two VCGs. First, *as spontaneous inner generosity* (VCG-01 — Exo35:5/22; 1Ch28:21; 2Ch29:31): the characteristic operates as the freely-moving heart/spirit that generates the motivation for offering and service from within — no external push is required; the inner movement is the source. Second, *as a divine-sustained orientation* (VCG-02 — Psa51:12): the characteristic operates as a disposition that must be received and upheld by God — the penitent cannot self-generate the willing spirit after failure; it must be divinely gifted. Both modes operate in the same constitutional location (heart/spirit) but with different constitutional sources (self-generated vs divinely-upheld).

---

##### T1.4.2 — Does this characteristic operate differently depending on context, direction, or constitutional level?

**[CHAR-1] Humility** (E)

Yes, the evidence shows context-dependent variation. *Direction-vertical (human-to-God):* the ka.na corpus is almost entirely vertical — humbling *before* God in response to his word or judgment. The Godward direction dominates the Hebrew register. *Direction-horizontal (human-to-human):* Phili2:3 (counting others more significant), Eph4:2 (foundational to corporate harmony), 1Pe5:5 (clothing oneself with humility toward one another) — the Greek register frequently operates in the horizontal direction. *Constitutional level variation:* the act of humbling (volitional, initiating — ka.na verbal) operates at the will level; the pervading disposition (tapeinofrosunē as character-garment) operates as a deeper character-level orientation; the condition of lowliness (tapeinōsis) operates as a state the person inhabits at multiple constitutional levels simultaneously. The characteristic is thus genuinely multi-directional and multi-level in its operation.

**[CHAR-2] Submission** (E)

Yes. *Direction-vertical (human-to-God):* the gospel-obedience cluster (M09-D-VCG-01 — Rom1:5; 16:26; Heb5:9; 1Pe1:2) is Godward; obedience of faith as submission to divine revelation and Christ's authority. *Direction-horizontal (human-to-authority structures):* household obedience patterns (M09-D-VCG-03 — Eph6:1; Col3:20; 1Ti3:4) and apostolic-communal obedience (Phili2:12; 2Th3:14) are horizontal. *Constitutional level:* the M09-C sub-group evidences heart-level and conscience-level operation (deep inner disposition); the M09-D sub-group evidences the surface-level relational pattern. The characteristic operates at different constitutional depths depending on whether it is being formed (Heb5:8 — deep, costly formation) or expressed as a known community characteristic (Rom16:19 — relational pattern publicly known).

**[CHAR-3] Contrition** (E)

The two-verse corpus does not directly evidence context-dependent variation. Both verses evidence the characteristic at the same constitutional levels (spirit and heart) and in the same relational direction (Godward). The Psa34:18 instance may be more crisis-precipitated (brokenhearted, crushed — acute states), while Isa57:15 presents the characteristic more as a sustained posture that God dwells with. The difference is between the acute moment of brokenness (Psa34:18 — crisis-precipitated) and the ongoing state that characterises the person (Isa57:15 — the one "who is of a contrite and lowly spirit"). Both registers are within the same characteristic; the variation is in temporal form (acute event vs ongoing condition) rather than direction or constitutional level.

**[CHAR-4] Meekness and gentleness** (E)

Yes. Metriopatheō (Heb5:2) operates in contexts of *pastoral/mediating engagement with the erring* — the characteristic is specifically engaged when the person must respond to waywardness or ignorance without being harsh. Okneō (Act9:38) operates in contexts of *responsive service to urgent need* — the characteristic is engaged when a genuine call for help requires immediate response. The two contextual modes differ in their direction: metriopatheō is engaged by encounter with another's moral condition; okneō is engaged by encounter with another's practical need. The constitutional level may also differ: metriopatheō involves deeper character-formation (grounded in shared vulnerability); okneō involves the will's kinetic readiness in the moment of response.

**[CHAR-5] Dignity** (E)

Yes, the three verses evidence the same inner quality expressing differently across three distinct contexts. In public civic life (1Ti2:2): semnotēs shapes the manner of inhabiting community existence — composure and peaceful gravity in the civic sphere. In household leadership (1Ti3:4): semnotēs produces ordered authority in the family — gravity as the source of respect-evoking leadership. In teaching (Tit2:7): semnotēs authenticates instruction — gravitas as the character-ground of credible teaching. The same inner quality expresses appropriately to each context without changing its essential nature.

**[CHAR-6] Willing-heartedness** (E)

Yes. In the tabernacle/temple worship contexts (Exo35:5/22; 1Ch28:21; 2Ch29:31), the characteristic operates as the positive freely-motivated orientation of the community toward God's service — the spontaneous generosity of those who are in good standing with God. In the penitential context (Psa51:12), the characteristic operates as a sought-restoration after moral failure — the person who has lost the spontaneous willing orientation prays for it to be divinely restored. The context thus determines whether the characteristic operates as a natural inner expression (VCG-01) or as a divinely-restored gift (VCG-02).

---

##### T1.4.3 — Does this characteristic have a communicative or speech-based mode of operation — and if so, how does it function?

**[CHAR-1] Humility** (E)

The evidence shows a limited but real speech-adjacent mode. In the ka.na corpus, the outward expression of the inner humbling is often paired with speech-acts: 2Ch12:6 — the princes "humble themselves and said, 'The Lord is righteous'" — verbal acknowledgment of God's rightness is the expression of the inner act. 2Ki22:19 and 2Ch34:27 pair the humbling with weeping and tearing clothes (embodied expression) but also with the act of receiving God's spoken word as addressed to oneself. The characteristic does not primarily operate through speech but it regularly issues in speech-acts of acknowledgment (confession, praise, verbal self-lowering) and is itself awakened by the reception of spoken word (God's address through the prophets). 2Cor10:1 (Paul's self-effacement in personal presence) suggests the characteristic also shapes the manner and register of speech rather than its content alone.

**[CHAR-2] Submission** (E)

Yes, in a specific form. 1Pe3:6 — Sarah's obedience to Abraham "expressed through speech and inward disposition (calling him lord)" — the characteristic finds verbal expression in the form of address and acknowledgment. The naming of Christ as Lord in 1Pe1:2 (obedience to Jesus Christ) and the confession of the gospel (2Cor9:13 — "submission that comes from your confession of the gospel of Christ") also evidence a speech-adjacent mode: confession and acknowledgment are the verbal expression of the inner submission of will. The characteristic also has an auditory mode at its origin: hupakoē (under-hearing) — the will's yielding is constitutively responsive to what is heard; submission operates through the inner hearing of the word and its claim.

**[CHAR-3] Contrition** (S)

The two-verse corpus does not directly evidence a speech-based mode of operation for contrition. The characteristic is evidenced as an inner state that God responds to, not as a verbal expression. Psalm 34 in its broader context involves praise and testimony, but within the two-verse evidence scope (Psa34:18 alone), the speech dimension is not addressed.

**[CHAR-4] Meekness and gentleness** (E)

Indirectly. The high priest's "dealing gently with the ignorant and wayward" (Heb5:2 — metriopatheō) almost certainly involves speech in its mediation — the priestly mediation between erring people and God involves verbal intercession and instruction. The gentleness of the engagement is carried partly through the manner and tone of speech. Act9:38 involves speech explicitly ("urging him, 'Please come to us without delay'") — but the speech here is the disciples' communication, not Peter's; the okneō named is the absence of the quality that would prevent prompt response. The characteristic shapes the manner of speech (the metriopatheō quality) without being a purely speech-constituted characteristic.

**[CHAR-5] Dignity** (E)

Yes. Tit2:7 — "in your teaching show integrity, dignity" — the characteristic operates directly in and through the teaching act; the manner of teaching carries the dignity. The speech-act of teaching is the domain in which semnotēs must be visible — the inner character quality shapes the manner and weight of the verbal instruction. Similarly, 1Ti3:4's household management involves communication (giving direction, correcting children) that must be carried with dignity rather than without it. The characteristic has a communicative mode in the sense that it shapes the quality and tone of how the person speaks and acts in social roles.

**[CHAR-6] Willing-heartedness** (S)

The five-verse corpus does not directly evidence a speech-based mode of operation for willing-heartedness. The characteristic is evidenced through physical acts of bringing offerings (Exo35; 2Ch29:31) and skilled service (1Ch28:21) rather than through verbal expression. Psa51:12's prayer is verbal but addresses the restoration of the characteristic rather than its exercise through speech.

---

#### T1.5 — Immediate Response

##### T1.5.1 — What is the first or most immediate inner-being response to receiving or encountering this characteristic?

**[CHAR-1] Humility** (E)

Where the corpus describes an immediate response, it is typically a relational opening toward God: in the ka.na narrative, the immediate correlate of humbling is God *hearing* the person — "I also have heard you" (2Ki22:19; 2Ch34:27). The inner movement is toward receptivity: the heart that was hardened or self-asserting becomes open to God's word, mercy, and address. In the NT register, the immediate relational opening is toward others: Eph4:2 positions humility as the disposition that enables bearing with one another; the tax collector in Luk18:14 goes home justified immediately following his posture of self-lowering before God. The characteristic thus produces an immediate relational opening — toward God in the OT register, toward divine justification/mercy in the NT. There is no evidence of delayed or deferred immediate response; the opening appears to be structurally immediate.

**[CHAR-2] Submission** (E)

Where the corpus addresses immediate response, two distinct patterns emerge by sub-group. In M09-C (inner disposition), the immediate inner response is a cognitive-volitional repositioning: the person arrives at an inner assessment that determines their self-understanding and allegiance — Luk17:10 ("we are unworthy servants; we have only done what was our duty") is the self-assessment that immediately follows doing what was commanded; Rom6:17 ("obedience from the heart") names the immediate inner state of genuine transformation. In M09-D (relational pattern), the immediate relational response is the visible obedient act: Act6:7 — priests becoming obedient to the faith as a decisive reorientation; 2Cor7:15 — the Corinthians' receptive welcome of Titus with fear and trembling as the immediate expression of their inner obedience.

**[CHAR-3] Contrition** (E)

The immediate inner-being response evidenced is God's response to the contrite state: Psa34:18 — "the Lord is near" and "saves the crushed in spirit"; Isa57:15 — "I dwell with him who is of a contrite and lowly spirit, to revive the spirit of the lowly, and to revive the heart of the contrite." The immediate divine response is drawing near, saving, dwelling, and reviving. From the human side, the immediate response that the corpus evidences is the opening of the inner person toward God's action — the crushed spirit is one that does not resist God's nearness but is open to receive it. The corpus does not evidence an immediate human-experienced feeling that precedes or follows the state; the focus is on the divine response to the state.

**[CHAR-4] Meekness and gentleness** (E)

The immediate responses are different for the two elements. Metriopatheō (Heb5:2) — the immediate inner response evidenced is the *engaged compassionate modulation* of one's manner toward the erring; the characteristic immediately shapes how the person responds to the erring person's condition. The erring person who encounters the high priest's metriopatheō receives a measured compassionate dealing rather than severity. Okneō (Act9:38) — the immediate inner response is *prompt movement without friction*: the person responding without hesitation immediately begins moving toward the caller's need. The characteristic is evidenced at the moment of call-and-response.

**[CHAR-5] Dignity** (E)

1Ti3:4 evidences the most direct immediate response: the overseer's dignity produces "orderly, respectful compliance in his children" — the immediate response of those who encounter semnotēs is a form of ordered respect rather than defiance or casual dismissal. The characteristic naturally evokes the response of appropriate regard in those who encounter it. This is not a demanded response (the dignity does not assert its claim) but an evoked one — the settled moral gravity of the person naturally generates the corresponding response of respect.

**[CHAR-6] Willing-heartedness** (E)

The most immediate response evidenced is *the act of bringing/giving* — the freely-moved heart immediately issues in the outward act of offering. Exo35:5 — "whoever is of a generous heart, let him bring" — the willing heart and the bringing are directly connected without intervening friction. Exo35:22 — "all who were of a willing heart brought" — the aorist verb confirms the immediacy: the willing-hearted ones brought. 1Ch28:21 — "every willing man who has skill for any kind of service" — the willingness directly enables participation. The characteristic is its own immediate activation — no further inner preparation is required; the willing heart simply brings.

---

##### T1.5.2 — Is the immediate response consistent across the verse evidence, or does it vary?

**[CHAR-1] Humility** (E)

The immediate response is broadly consistent in direction (relational opening and receptivity) but varies in target and register. In the ka.na corpus, the immediate correlate is consistently divine perception and responsive mercy — God sees the humbling and responds (1Ki21:29; 2Ch12:7; 2Ch33:12). In the tapeinoō corpus, the immediate correlate is divine exaltation or justification (Luk18:14; Jam4:10; 1Pe5:6). In the tapeinofrosunē corpus, the immediate relational effect is community harmony and genuine regard for others (Phili2:3; Eph4:2). The variation is in the *target* of the opening (God's mercy, divine exaltation, or human regard) rather than in the structural character of the response (relational opening). The consistency across the variation is the directional movement from self-enclosure to openness.

**[CHAR-2] Submission** (E)

The response varies in form between the two sub-groups but is consistent in direction: both M09-C and M09-D evidence an inner repositioning of the will toward the authority. What varies is the constitutional level at which the response is named — M09-C names the heart and conscience level; M09-D names the relational pattern level. The common direction is the will's yielding rather than its self-assertion, whether that is expressed as inner self-assessment (Luk17:10), heart-conformity (Rom6:17), sincere-heart service (Eph6:5; Col3:22), or decisive community-level reorientation (Act6:7; Rom16:19).

**[CHAR-3] Contrition** (E)

The two-verse corpus is consistent: in both verses, the immediate divine response to contrition is presence and restoration (nearness + saving in Psa34:18; dwelling + reviving in Isa57:15). The direction and quality of divine response is consistent: God moves toward the contrite rather than away. The verbs vary (near, saves, dwells, revives) but all describe a consistent divine orientation of drawing near and restoring.

**[CHAR-4] Meekness and gentleness** (E)

The two terms evidence different immediate responses (calibrated compassionate engagement vs prompt movement), consistent with the two-element structure of the characteristic (T1.2.2). Within each term's evidence, the response is consistent: metriopatheō produces the same calibrated-gentle quality each time it is active; okneō (in its absence) names the friction that delays immediate response. The variation is between the two elements rather than within either.

**[CHAR-5] Dignity** (E)

The response pattern is consistent across contexts: semnotēs produces ordered, respectful engagement in those who encounter it. 1Ti3:4 — orderly compliance from children; 1Ti2:2 — peaceful ordered public life as the context in which dignity operates (implying its absence would produce disorder); Tit2:7 — teaching authenticated by dignity as one that hearers can receive as genuinely weighted. In all three contexts, the characteristic's presence produces ordered, respectful engagement rather than dismissive disregard.

**[CHAR-6] Willing-heartedness** (E)

Consistent in VCG-01: in all four offering/service verses (Exo35:5/22; 1Ch28:21; 2Ch29:31), the immediate response is the outward act of bringing or serving — the willing heart produces the action without recorded inner conflict or delay. In VCG-02 (Psa51:12), the dynamic is different: the psalmist's immediate response is the prayer for restoration of the willing spirit, not the act of free giving. The VCG-01 pattern (immediate active giving) and VCG-02 pattern (immediate prayerful seeking) are consistent within their respective registers but different between registers.

---

##### T1.5.3 — Where the verse evidence is silent on immediate response, note this explicitly.

**[CHAR-1] Humility** (E)

The evidence addresses immediate response extensively. The M09-B register (experienced lowliness — 2Cor7:6; 2Ch28:19; Psa107:12) is the partial exception: the imposed-lowering verses describe the state of being brought low but do not always record an immediate inner response from the person who is lowered. 2Cor7:6 ("God who comforts the downcast, comforted us by the coming of Titus") names God's response to the state but the person's immediate inner response is implicit rather than named. This partial silence is analytically significant: in the imposed-lowering register, the inner response is not automatically generated — the person may experience the lowered state without the relational opening that characterises the voluntary register. The two registers thus differ in their immediate response profile.

**[CHAR-2] Submission** (E)

The evidence is not broadly silent on immediate response (see T1.5.1 and T1.5.2). However, the eschatological instances (Gen49:10 — future gathering of nations; 1Pe1:2 — goal of the Spirit's sanctifying work) describe the characteristic as a future telos rather than an immediate response to present encounter; their immediate-response profile is implied rather than named.

**[CHAR-3] Contrition** (E)

The corpus addresses the divine immediate response extensively (see T1.5.1 and T1.5.2) but is silent on the human-experienced immediate inner response *to* the state of contrition — what it feels like to be in the state, or what immediately precedes or accompanies it from the human experiential side. The two-verse corpus focuses on what God does in response rather than on the human experiential phenomenology of the state itself.

**[CHAR-4] Meekness and gentleness** (S)

The inner experiential response of the person who possesses the characteristic (what meekness/gentleness feels like from the inside) is not directly addressed in the two-verse corpus. The evidence names the characteristic's outward quality (dealing gently with the erring; coming without delay) rather than the phenomenological inner experience of the person who has it.

**[CHAR-5] Dignity** (E)

The immediate response of the bearer of the characteristic (what it feels like to have semnotēs) is not directly addressed — the evidence describes the effect on others (orderly compliance, authenticated teaching, peaceable life) rather than the inner phenomenology of possessing the characteristic.

**[CHAR-6] Willing-heartedness** (E)

The inner phenomenology of the person exercising the characteristic (what willing-heartedness feels like from the inside) is not directly addressed. The evidence describes the outer acts produced by the inner disposition rather than the felt inner experience of the person who is willing-hearted.

---

#### T1.6 — Sustained Effect

##### T1.6.1 — What does this characteristic produce in the inner being over time?

**[CHAR-1] Humility** (E)

The sustained operation of humility produces several inner-being outcomes across the corpus. (a) *Wisdom:* Pro11:2 — "with the humble is wisdom" — sustained humility as the inner disposition that opens the person to true understanding. (b) *Contentment:* Phili4:12 — Paul has "learned the secret of facing plenty and hunger" through repeated experience of being brought low (tapeinoō); the sustained navigating of lowliness produces a cultivated stability. (c) *Corporate harmony:* Eph4:2 — humility as the foundational disposition enabling the sustained bearing of one another in love. (d) *Divine favour:* the sustained pattern across the ka.na corpus is that sustained humility before God is the inner condition that maintains the covenantal relationship and averts judgment. (e) *Character formation:* Col3:12 — humility is a garment deliberately put on as part of the character-wardrobe of the renewed person; sustained wearing shapes the whole character.

**[CHAR-2] Submission** (E)

The corpus evidences several sustained inner-being productions. (a) *Soul-purification:* 1Pe1:22 — "having purified your souls by your obedience to the truth" — sustained obedience to truth effects inner purification of the soul's deepest faculty. (b) *Sincere brotherly love:* 1Pe1:22 — the same verse evidences that purification through obedience produces "sincere brotherly love from a pure heart" — love is the fruit of sustained submission to truth. (c) *Learned costly dispositionality:* Heb5:8 — Christ learned obedience through suffering — the sustained engagement with authority even through cost shapes the disposition; Phili2:12 ("always obeyed") evidences the sustained disposition that outlasts the external support of apostolic presence. (d) *Reliable character-orientation:* Phile21 — Paul's confidence in Philemon's obedience as a reliable inner orientation "toward doing good beyond the minimum" — sustained submission produces trustworthy character.

**[CHAR-3] Contrition** (E)

The sustained production evidenced is: *reviving of spirit and heart* (Isa57:15 — "to revive the spirit of the lowly, and to revive the heart of the contrite"). The characteristic produces, through God's reviving response, a renewal of the inner person that the crushed state itself cannot produce — God's dwelling with the contrite is what generates the reviving. The long-term product of genuine contrition (as God responds to it) is thus the renewal and restoration of the spirit and heart that were crushed. Saving action (Psa34:18) implies a delivered state that follows the crushed state through God's intervention.

**[CHAR-4] Meekness and gentleness** (S)

The two-verse corpus does not directly address the long-term productions of meekness and gentleness. Heb5:2 addresses the present capacity for gentle dealing; Act9:38 addresses the present quality of volitional readiness. Sustained long-term productions are not directly evidenced in these two verses.

**[CHAR-5] Dignity** (E)

The sustained production evidenced is the ordering of the person's social existence and relationships. 1Ti2:2 — peaceful and quiet life as the sustained condition that dignity enables; 1Ti3:4 — orderly household as the sustained outcome of the overseer's semnotēs; Tit2:7 — sustained credibility of teaching as the long-term fruit of consistent dignity alongside integrity. The characteristic produces sustained ordered-existence rather than periodic relational crises caused by the absence of settled moral gravity.

**[CHAR-6] Willing-heartedness** (E)

2Ch29:31 — "all who were of a willing heart brought burnt offerings" — this is within Hezekiah's reform context, suggesting the restoration of worship produces renewed willing-heartedness in the community; sustained worship and willing offering are mutually reinforcing. Psa51:12 — "uphold me with a willing spirit" — implicitly names what sustained possession of the characteristic would produce: the ability to sustain joyful, freely-offered devotion over time. The sustained production is *habitual generous offering and wholehearted service* — the willing-hearted person becomes characterologically generous and devoted over time.

---

##### T1.6.2 — What states, qualities, capacities, or orientations does sustained exposure to or possession of this characteristic establish?

**[CHAR-1] Humility** (E)

The corpus evidences the following established states and qualities. *Relational openness:* sustained humility before God (the ka.na pattern) establishes and maintains the openness that characterises genuine covenant relation — the person who habitually humbles is consistently accessible to God's word and responsive to his address. *Self-knowledge:* 2Ch32:26 (Hezekiah humbling in response to "the pride of his heart") evidences that humility produces accurate inner self-assessment — the person who humbles genuinely is one who can identify their own fault. *Security:* Mat11:29 — Christ's own lowliness-in-heart is what makes his yoke restful ("you will find rest for your souls"); sustained possession of the characteristic produces rest rather than the anxiety of self-maintenance and status-protection. *Teachability:* Act20:19 — Paul's sustained humility through trials maintains his ministry posture; sustained humility sustains openness to correction and formation.

**[CHAR-2] Submission** (E)

The corpus evidences the following sustained states: *Inward purity:* 1Pe1:22 — the soul purified by obedience to truth; *Love-capacity:* 1Pe1:22 — the love that flows from the pure heart formed by sustained obedience; *Fear of God:* Phili2:12 ("with fear and trembling") and Col3:22 ("fearing the Lord") — sustained submission cultivates the reverential posture toward God that is the foundation of genuine relationship; *Wise discernment:* Rom16:19 — Paul redirects the Romans' obedient disposition toward "wisdom as to what is good and innocence as to what is evil" — sustained obedience creates the capacity for discernment; *Identity-formation:* 1Pe1:14 — obedience defines the believer's identity as "obedient children," shaping the will's structural opposition to "former passions."

**[CHAR-3] Contrition** (E)

The two-verse corpus is thin on sustained states beyond the immediate reviving. Isa57:15 — the revived spirit and heart — names restored vitality as the state that contrition occasions through God's response. The pairing of contrition with lowliness (Isa57:15) suggests that the sustained orientation following genuine contrition includes the lowly posture of CHAR-1 — the person who has been genuinely broken does not return to prideful self-sufficiency but retains a lowered relational posture toward God. However, this is inferential from the pairing rather than directly stated.

**[CHAR-4] Meekness and gentleness** (E)

From the evidence available: Heb5:2's grounding of metriopatheō in shared creaturely weakness suggests that sustained possession of the characteristic is associated with sustained self-knowledge of one's own limitation — the capacity for gentleness is maintained by the continuing awareness of one's own weakness. This suggests that sustained possession establishes a self-aware-and-other-oriented inner orientation: the person who genuinely possesses the characteristic over time is one who remains genuinely aware of their own creaturely limitation. Act9:38's okneō absence suggests sustained possession establishes a habitual readiness-without-friction as a character quality.

**[CHAR-5] Dignity** (E)

The sustained possession of semnotēs establishes: *composure in public existence* (1Ti2:2 — the person of settled dignity inhabits public life without anxiety-driven self-assertion); *household order through character* (1Ti3:4 — order sustained by character gravity rather than force); *teaching credibility* (Tit2:7 — instruction that carries the authentic weight of the teacher's character over time). Together: a sustained ordering influence on the social contexts the person inhabits, produced by the consistent inner quality rather than by external enforcement.

**[CHAR-6] Willing-heartedness** (E)

The corpus implies that sustained willing-heartedness establishes: *habitual generosity* (Exo35:5/22 — repeated free-will offering as the pattern of the willing-hearted community); *wholehearted skilled service* (1Ch28:21 — willingness enabling full engagement of skill); *sustained joyful devotion* (Psa51:12 — the willing spirit as the condition for joy-of-salvation and freely-offered devotion). Together: a stable orientation of the whole person toward freely-given service and worship without the friction of reluctance or compulsion.

---

##### T1.6.3 — Does the sustained effect differ from the immediate response — and if so, how?

**[CHAR-1] Humility** (E)

Yes. The immediate response (T1.5.1) is primarily relational opening — the moment of humbling produces immediate access to God's mercy or divine exaltation. The sustained effect differs in kind: it is characterological rather than eventive. Phili4:12 ("I have *learned* the secret of facing… being brought low") evidences that sustained navigation of the lowliness condition produces a *learned* stability — a cultivated inner quality rather than a moment of opening. Col3:12 (humility as a "put-on" character-garment) evidences that sustained voluntary humbling shapes the whole inner person's character-wardrobe, not merely producing isolated moments of divine access. Pro11:2 (humility → wisdom) evidences that sustained humility produces a deepened inner capacity (wisdom) rather than merely repeated moments of receptivity. The sustained effect is the characterological deposit of the repeated inner act.

**[CHAR-2] Submission** (E)

Yes. The immediate response (T1.5.1) is the repositioning of the will — a volitional act or inner self-assessment that follows the encounter with the authority's claim. The sustained effect is *characterological depth*: sustained obedience purifies the soul (1Pe1:22 — deeper than the volitional act), produces love (1Pe1:22 — an affective capacity), and creates the identity of "obedient children" (1Pe1:14 — a stable self-understanding). The progression is from act → disposition → identity → affective capacity. The immediate response is an inner positioning; the sustained effect is an inner formation that becomes character.

**[CHAR-3] Contrition** (E)

Yes. The immediate divine response is drawing near and saving (Psa34:18 — present-tense action). The sustained effect is the reviving of spirit and heart (Isa57:15 — a deeper renewal that goes beyond the moment of nearness to a thoroughgoing restoration of inner vitality). The immediate response is relational presence; the sustained effect is constitutional renewal. The characteristic thus opens the person to God's presence (immediate) which then produces the deeper work of reviving (sustained).

**[CHAR-4] Meekness and gentleness** (S)

The two-verse corpus does not directly evidence the distinction between immediate response and sustained effect for this characteristic. The evidence addresses the characteristic as a present capacity and quality without describing its developmental arc over time.

**[CHAR-5] Dignity** (E)

Yes. The immediate response (T1.5.1) is the immediate ordered-respect evoked by encounter with the characteristic. The sustained effect is the long-term ordering of the person's social spheres — household order (1Ti3:4), peaceful public life (1Ti2:2), teaching credibility (Tit2:7) — all sustained over time through the consistent inner quality. The immediate evocation of respect is the momentary expression; the sustained ordering of social spheres is the long-term fruit.

**[CHAR-6] Willing-heartedness** (E)

Yes. The immediate response (T1.5.1) is the specific act of bringing or serving prompted by the freely-moved inner orientation. The sustained effect is the characterological formation of a generous, devout person who habitually brings and serves from an inner that is consistently freely-moved. The immediate response is event-level; the sustained effect is character-level — the person becomes the willing-hearted person through repeated free-offering rather than merely performing willing-hearted acts on specific occasions.

---

#### T1.7 — Conditions of Reception

##### T1.7.1 — What inner conditions or orientations enable genuine reception of this characteristic?

**[CHAR-1] Humility** (E)

The corpus evidences several enabling inner conditions. (a) *Heart-tenderness:* 2Ki22:19 and 2Ch34:27 both pair humbling with a "penitent" and "tender" heart — the person who can hear God's word as addressed to themselves, affecting them. (b) *Hearing God's word as personally relevant:* in 2Ki22:19 and 2Ch34:27, the humbling is explicitly triggered by hearing God's words "against this place and its inhabitants" — the inner condition that enables reception is the capacity to hear universal divine address as personally claiming. (c) *Awareness of one's own fault:* 2Ch32:26 — Hezekiah's humbling responds to an identified inner fault ("pride of his heart"); 2Ch33:12 — Manasseh's humbling comes in distress that his earlier faithlessness has produced. (d) *Absence of self-enclosure:* the opposite of what enables humility is named in 2Ch36:12 (refusal to humble = inner root of evil) and Col2:18 (puffed up sensuous mind), suggesting that genuine reception requires the person not to be closed into themselves.

**[CHAR-2] Submission** (E)

The corpus evidences several enabling inner conditions. (a) *Fear of God / reverential orientation:* Phili2:12 ("fear and trembling") and Col3:22 ("fearing the Lord") — the reverence that places God's authority above social pressure. (b) *Sincere-heart genuineness:* Eph6:5; Col3:22 — the undivided, undistorted inner motive that does not split between inner compliance and outward performance. (c) *Faith:* Heb11:8 — Abraham obeyed "by faith" — trusting the divine word more than personal comprehension; the obedience of faith (Rom1:5; 16:26) is enabled by the trust-orientation of faith. (d) *Conscience-function:* Phili2:12 (sustained without external accountability — conscience and fear of God replace social pressure) — the conscience functioning as the internal accountability when external accountability is removed.

**[CHAR-3] Contrition** (E)

The corpus evidences one primary enabling condition: the openness to genuine moral self-confrontation rather than self-defence. Both verses evidence the person who genuinely registers their own broken condition — "the brokenhearted" (Psa34:18) and "the contrite" (Isa57:15) are presented as those who have not fled from the reality of their inner state. The enabling condition is thus the absence of the self-protective deflection that would prevent the characteristic from taking hold. Additionally, Isa57:15's pairing with "lowly" suggests that the lowly posture (CHAR-1 — openness to God's authority and word) is a pre-condition for genuine contrition to emerge rather than hardened self-justification.

**[CHAR-4] Meekness and gentleness** (E)

Heb5:2 directly evidences an enabling inner condition: *genuine awareness of one's own shared creaturely weakness*. The high priest "can deal gently with the ignorant and wayward, since he himself is beset with weakness" — the since (Greek: kathoti) establishes a causal relationship: the capacity for gentleness is enabled by the genuine self-awareness of shared limitation. The person who has not genuinely reckoned with their own weakness cannot access the ground for metriopatheō — they approach the erring from an assumed superiority that precludes measured compassionate restraint.

**[CHAR-5] Dignity** (S)

The three-verse corpus does not directly address the inner conditions that enable a person to receive or develop semnotēs. The verses address the characteristic as already present in the persons described rather than naming the conditions for its formation.

**[CHAR-6] Willing-heartedness** (E)

The corpus evidences two enabling conditions. First, *genuine inner-motivated orientation toward God*: Exo35:5 — "whoever is of a generous/willing heart" — the heart's own genuine movement toward God rather than external compliance. The enabling condition is that the inner movement originates genuinely in the person's own heart rather than being externally produced. Second, *reception as divine gift* (Psa51:12 — "uphold me with a willing spirit"): the enabling condition for the restoration of willing-heartedness after failure is divine upholding — the person must receive it from God rather than self-generate it. The enabling condition varies by register: in VCG-01, the enabling is genuine inner freedom; in VCG-02, the enabling is divine gift-reception.

---

##### T1.7.2 — What inner conditions block, distort, or prevent reception?

**[CHAR-1] Humility** (E)

The corpus names blocking conditions explicitly. (a) *Pride and self-exaltation:* the structural opposite throughout — Mat23:12 contrasts self-exaltation and self-humbling as the blocking and enabling conditions respectively. (b) *Hardness of heart:* Lev26:41 — the "uncircumcised heart" must be broken for the humbling to occur; heart-hardness blocks the inner movement. (c) *Performance and self-referential asceticism:* Col2:18 and Col2:23 evidence that self-imposed severity which keeps the self at the centre (puffed up, self-made religion) distorts the characteristic even while producing its outward appearance. (d) *Political or social resistance:* 2Ch30:11 — "some men of Asher, of Manasseh, and of Zebulun humbled themselves and came to Jerusalem" — implying that many did not; the inner yielding that overcame tribal resistance was the condition for response, while those who refused were blocked by the same social-political attachment. (e) *Moral accumulation:* 2Ch33:23 — Amon's increasing guilt is linked to his persistent non-humbling; the refusal becomes self-reinforcing.

**[CHAR-2] Submission** (E)

The corpus names several blocking conditions. (a) *Eye-service and people-pleasing:* Eph6:5; Col3:22 — the outward-performance orientation that substitutes social approval for genuine inner compliance; this distorts submission into performance. (b) *Self-assertion and inner refusal:* 2Th1:8 — "not knowing God" paired with disobedience suggests that genuine self-assertion against God's revealed authority blocks reception. (c) *Deference to false authority:* Gal2:5 — Paul's deliberate non-submission evidences that yielding to illegitimate authority would be a distortion rather than an expression of the characteristic; discernment of legitimate authority is a prerequisite. (d) *Yielding to bodily passions:* Rom6:12 — sin's reign producing obedience to bodily desires is the competing inner yielding that blocks submission to God; the characteristic is blocked by the competing submission to desire.

**[CHAR-3] Contrition** (E)

The structural opposites evidenced by implication are: *pride and hardness* — the elevated, self-sufficient spirit that does not register the weight of moral failure; *self-justification* — the inner defence that interprets the person's own condition as not-requiring-breaking; *hardening* — the refusal to allow the inner collapse that genuine contrition requires. The God who "dwells with the contrite" (Isa57:15) does not similarly dwell with the hardened or proud — implying that hardness and pride block the characteristic's reception by blocking the inner-collapse it requires.

**[CHAR-4] Meekness and gentleness** (E)

The blocking conditions implied by Heb5:2 are: *prideful distance from one's own weakness* — the person who has not genuinely confronted their own creaturely limitation approaches the erring with severity rather than measured gentleness. The structural opposite of "beset with weakness" (the self-knowledge that grounds gentleness) is the self-presentation of one who has not acknowledged their own limitation. For okneō (Act9:38), the blocking condition is *self-protective reluctance or hesitation* — the inward resistance that generates friction between perceiving a need and responding to it.

**[CHAR-5] Dignity** (E)

The structural opposite (M08 — proud self-display) implies the primary blocking condition: when the person's inner orientation is toward self-promotion, status-assertion, and performance of worth, the settled inner gravity of semnotēs cannot take hold. Proud self-display (M08 register) blocks the self-possession and non-assertive gravity that semnotēs names. Additionally, frivolousness or lack of moral seriousness would prevent the characteristic from forming — the person whose manner is light or self-trivialising cannot carry the sober weighty gravity of semnotēs.

**[CHAR-6] Willing-heartedness** (E)

The corpus implies several blocking conditions. *Compulsion and external pressure*: the free-offering register (Exo35:5/22) implies that external compulsion blocks genuine willing-heartedness — commanded giving cannot produce willing-heartedness, only compliance. *Moral failure*: Psa51:12 — the request for restoration implies that moral failure disrupts or destroys the willing orientation, leaving the person unable to freely offer until the spirit is divinely restored. *Guilt and spiritual heaviness*: Psa51:12's penitential context implies that the burden of unresolved failure blocks the free movement of the spirit.

---

##### T1.7.3 — What is the inner-being state of the person who encounters this characteristic but does not receive it?

**[CHAR-1] Humility** (E)

The corpus describes this state across the negative instances. The non-humbling person is in a condition of *increasing guilt* (2Ch33:23 — Amon), *inner rootedness in evil* (2Ch36:12 — Zedekiah's refusal as the root of his evil), and *self-enclosure against God's word* (2Ch36:12 — refusing to humble before the prophet who spoke from God's mouth). The Phili2:3 contrast frames the non-humbling person as ruled by "selfish ambition and conceit" — cognitive self-assertion rather than other-regard. The Colossian material (Col2:18) identifies the distorted inner state as being "puffed up without reason by his sensuous mind" — an inflated inner condition that mistakes performance for transformation. The inner-being state of the non-receiver is thus pride-in-operation: self-enclosed, self-elevating, resistant to both divine address and genuine other-regard.

**[CHAR-2] Submission** (E)

The corpus describes this across several negative instances. 2Th1:8 — those who "do not obey the gospel" are in a state of "not knowing God" — relational rupture at the deepest level, with catastrophic eschatological consequence. 2Th3:14 — the one who refuses to obey apostolic instruction reveals "an inner refusal" that the communal response is designed to awaken to repentance — the blocking state is refusal, not merely ignorance. Pro30:17 — the eye that scorns to obey a mother is in a state of contemptuous dismissal of legitimate authority — a moral condition the text marks with severe consequence. The inner state of the non-receiver is consistent: some form of self-assertion or inner refusal that places the person's own judgment or desire above the legitimate authority's claim.

**[CHAR-3] Contrition** (S)

The two-verse corpus does not directly name the inner state of the person who encounters the occasion for contrition but does not enter it. The evidence describes those who are contrite (the recipients of God's nearness and reviving) without describing those who refuse contrition. The structural implication is hardness, self-sufficiency, or unrepentance — but this is inferred from the contrast structure rather than directly evidenced in §3.B.

**[CHAR-4] Meekness and gentleness** (S)

The two-verse corpus does not directly describe the inner state of the person who fails to receive or embody this characteristic. The structural opposites (severity, hesitation) are implied but not described as inner states of specific persons.

**[CHAR-5] Dignity** (S)

The three-verse corpus does not directly describe the inner state of the person who encounters semnotēs but does not receive or embody it. The evidence describes the characteristic in those who have it rather than those who lack it.

**[CHAR-6] Willing-heartedness** (S)

The corpus does not directly name the inner state of the person who encounters the occasion for willing-heartedness but whose heart is not willing. Exo35:5 implies that some in the community are not of a willing heart (only "whoever is of a willing heart" is called to bring) but does not describe those whose hearts are not willing. Psa51:12 indirectly names the unwilling-spirit state as the condition requiring divine restoration — but this is specifically post-failure, not a general description of the non-willing person.

---

#### T1.8 — Dimension Classification

##### T1.8.1 — What is the primary inner-being dimension of this characteristic from the programme's dimension vocabulary?

**[CHAR-1] Humility** (E)

Based on the evidence, the primary dimension is *volitional-dispositional*: humility is primarily the inner posture and orientation of the will. The dominant evidence is the ka.na verbal corpus (the person *humbles* themselves — a willed act), the tapeinoō corpus (Jam4:10; 1Pe5:6 — "humble yourselves" — imperative addressed to the will), and the tapeinofrosunē corpus (Phili2:3 — humility as the cognitive-volitional act of counting others more significant; Col3:12 — humility as a garment *put on* by choice). The characteristic is fundamentally an orientation of the will — toward God, toward others, and away from self-assertion — that both initiates inner events (acts of humbling) and constitutes the stable inner character (disposition of humility).

**[CHAR-2] Submission** (E)

The primary dimension is *volitional*: submission is constitutively the will's act and disposition. The Romans evidence is the clearest: Rom6:16/17 — the will's habitual yielding to sin or to righteousness determines whose servant one is; Rom6:17 — obedience "from the heart" is the will's genuine conformity. The imperative form throughout ("obey," "be subject") addresses the volitional capacity. Gal2:5 — the act of submission can be withheld — confirms it is the will's act. Phili2:12 — sustained without external support — confirms it is the will's disposition. The volitional dimension is primary because without the will's genuine engagement, what remains is performance (the M09-C/D split itself is premised on this: M09-C carries the genuine inner-disposition face precisely because genuine will-submission cannot be reduced to M09-D's relational pattern alone).

**[CHAR-3] Contrition** (E)

The primary dimension is *affective-existential*: the characteristic is primarily an intense inner experience of brokenness — the heart broken, the spirit crushed (Psa34:18). This is not primarily a volitional act (the person does not choose to be crushed in the way they choose to humble themselves), nor primarily a cognitive evaluation, but an acute inner suffering before God — an existential condition of the inner person's collapse. The affective-existential dimension — the state of being inwardly broken and crushed — is the primary constitutional character of contrition.

**[CHAR-4] Meekness and gentleness** (E)

The primary dimension is *volitional-character*: the characteristic is primarily the quality of how the will engages and responds. Metriopatheō (Heb5:2) — "can deal gently" — a capacity of the will governing its manner of engagement. Okneō (Act9:38) — the volitional readiness as the absence of inner resistance. Both terms evidence the will's quality and character as the primary constitutional location: the will that engages with measured calibration and without friction constitutes the characteristic. The volitional dimension is primary; the affective (compassion grounded in shared weakness) and cognitive (the self-assessment of one's own limitation) are enabling secondary dimensions.

**[CHAR-5] Dignity** (E)

The primary dimension is *characterological* — the stable quality of the person's character that shapes all their relational and social existence. Semnotēs is not primarily an act, a volitional event, or an acute state, but a settled character-quality that the person is: 1Ti2:2 — the inner quality characterising the whole manner of life; 1Ti3:4 — the character from which governance flows; Tit2:7 — the quality of teaching character. The primary dimension is the person's whole-character gravity rather than a single faculty's operation.

**[CHAR-6] Willing-heartedness** (E)

The primary dimension is *volitional-affective* — the freely-moving inner orientation of the will combined with the generous, joyful inner quality of the movement. The characteristic is primarily about how the will moves (freely, without compulsion) and the inner quality of that movement (generous, willing, gladly — not dragged). Both elements are present: the volitional (the will's free movement) and the affective (the generous, joyful quality of the movement — Psa51:12 pairs the willing spirit with the joy of salvation). The two together constitute the primary dimension: the will that gladly and freely moves toward God and service.

---

##### T1.8.2 — What evidence from the verse evidence supports this classification?

**[CHAR-1] Humility** (E)

Support for the volitional-dispositional primary dimension: Mat18:4 ("whoever humbles himself" — reflexive volitional act); Jam4:10 ("humble yourselves" — direct imperative to the will); 1Pe5:6 ("humble yourselves therefore under the mighty hand of God" — deliberate act of self-placement under divine sovereignty); Phili2:3 ("in humility count others more significant" — the cognitive-volitional reordering); Phili2:8 (Christ "humbled himself by becoming obedient" — the most extreme volitional act in the corpus); Col3:12 ("put on… humility" — the deliberate character-garment); Act20:19 ("serving the Lord with all humility" — the pervading volitional orientation of ministry). These together evidence that the primary constitutional engagement is the will's orientation and act.

**[CHAR-2] Submission** (E)

Supporting evidence for the volitional-primary classification: Rom6:16/17 — the will's yielding determines the enslaved condition; Rom6:12 — believers commanded to resist the inner yielding to bodily passions (addressing the will's directional capacity); Heb11:8 — Abraham "obeyed" as a faith-driven inner response (volitional); Gal2:5 — non-submission as a decisive volitional act; Phili2:12 — the sustained inner disposition rooted in conscience without social support (will functioning without external enablement); 1Ti2:11 — the willing, whole-hearted receptivity of the learner's inner posture (volitional orientation). All these evidence the will as the primary constitutional faculty engaged.

**[CHAR-3] Contrition** (E)

Supporting evidence: Psa34:18 — "brokenhearted" (nishbar-lev — the heart broken/shattered) and "crushed in spirit" (dak.ka-ruach — the spirit crushed/ground); these are acute experiential-constitutional states, not cognitive-volitional acts. Isa57:15 — "contrite and lowly spirit" (dak.ka-ve-shpal-ruach) — the spirit characterised by crushing and lowness as its state. The physical-resonance vocabulary (crushed, broken, pulverised) confirms that the primary dimension is the affective-experiential condition of intense inner suffering before God, not the volitional posture (CHAR-1 Humility) or the cognitive-evaluative act.

**[CHAR-4] Meekness and gentleness** (E)

Metriopatheō (Heb5:2) — "he can deal gently" — the capacity verb (can/dunamai) addresses a volitional capacity; dealing gently is something the will does in its engagement. Okneō (Act9:38) — "come without delay" — the readiness to act without friction is the will's kinetic quality. Both terms evidence the will as the primary constitutional faculty engaged — the characteristic is what the will does (engaged with measured compassion) and how the will moves (without hesitation).

**[CHAR-5] Dignity** (E)

Supporting evidence: 1Ti2:2 — "dignified in every way" — the scope is comprehensive, not confined to specific acts; 1Ti3:4 — "with all dignity" — the comprehensiveness again; "from which household governance flows" — governance is downstream of the character quality, confirming it as the upstream characterological source; Tit2:7 — listed alongside integrity as a quality of teaching character. All three evidence the characteristic as a stable, comprehensive, upstream character quality rather than a situational act or state.

**[CHAR-6] Willing-heartedness** (E)

Volitional support: Exo35:5 — "whoever is of a generous/willing heart let him bring" — the will's free movement toward the act; 1Ch28:21 — "every willing man" — the person characterised by their will's orientation; Psa51:12 — "a willing spirit" — the spirit's volitional quality. Affective support: 2Ch29:31 — "willing heart" in the context of festive worship restoration (joyful context); Psa51:12 — "uphold me with a willing spirit" immediately following "restore to me the joy of your salvation" — the willing spirit is paired directly with joy, confirming the affective dimension.

---

##### T1.8.3 — Does this characteristic carry secondary dimensions — and if so, what are they, and do they compete with the primary classification?

**[CHAR-1] Humility** (E)
