This grammatical range reveals that the characteristic simultaneously names a *person-type* (the humble person — tapeinos), an *act* (the humbling — tapeinoō/ka.na), a *character-disposition* (tapeinofrosunē), and a *state* (tapeinōsis) — the full inner-being spectrum of event, quality, disposition, and condition.

**[CHAR-2] Submission** (E)

Hupakoē (noun) names the quality and state of the inner posture of submission; hupakouō (verb) names the act of obeying. Hupotagē (noun) names the condition of being ordered under. Together the grammatical range covers: *noun of quality* (hupakoē — the characteristic as an inner quality; Rom1:5 "obedience of faith"), *verbal act* (hupakouō — the event of obeying; Heb11:8 "Abraham obeyed"), *noun of condition* (hupotagē — the state of being in submission; Gal2:5 "yield in submission"). The grammatical range reveals that submission operates as a quality the person has, an act the person performs, and a condition the person inhabits — the same three registers as Humility (CHAR-1), but with the relational-authority axis central throughout.

**[CHAR-3] Contrition** (E)

Dak.ka in this corpus functions as: *noun* (Psa34:18 — "the crushed in spirit" — naming the person characterised by the state) and *adjective* (Isa57:15 — "contrite and lowly spirit" — qualifying the spirit). The grammatical range is narrower than CHAR-1 or CHAR-2 — no verbal form (act of becoming contrite) or abstract noun (contrition as a quality) appears in the two-verse corpus. The grammatical range reveals that the characteristic is evidenced primarily as a *state* (the person is in this condition) and a *quality of the spirit* (the spirit has this character) rather than as an act one performs. This is consistent with T1.2.1's finding that contrition is primarily a condition rather than an act.

**[CHAR-4] Meekness and gentleness** (E)

Metriopatheō (Heb5:2) is an *infinitive* — "can deal gently" (dunatai metriopatheō): the infinitive expressing a capacity or ability of the high priest. The infinitive form reveals the characteristic as a *capacity of the will* rather than a habitual noun or adjectival descriptor. The subject "can" (dunamai) + metriopatheō structures it as an inner capacity-to-act rather than a possessed quality (noun) or a continuous state (participle). Okneō (Act9:38) appears in an imperatival context as its absence — "without delay/okneō" — functioning adverbially to describe the manner of the expected action. The grammatical range together covers: *capacity to act* (metriopatheō infinitive) and *manner of willing action* (okneō absence as adverbial qualifier). The range reveals the characteristic as primarily about how the will acts and what it can do.

**[CHAR-5] Dignity** (E)

Semnotēs appears in the corpus as a *noun* (the abstract quality — "dignity" as a named character quality) in all three verses. The adjectival form semnos appears elsewhere in the NT (Phil4:8 — "whatever is honorable"; Tit2:2 — "dignified" applied to older men) but is not in this §3.D corpus. The noun-only range reveals the characteristic as primarily named as an *abstract quality or virtue* rather than as an act or event — it is something the person has rather than something they do or a state they find themselves in. The noun form is consistent with the characterological-primary classification (T1.8.1): semnotēs names the stable character quality.

**[CHAR-6] Willing-heartedness** (E)

Na.div functions in the corpus as: *adjective* (Exo35:5 — "willing/generous heart" — qualifying the heart); *adjective/noun* (Exo35:22 — "of a willing heart" — the willing-hearted as a person-type); *person-type noun* (1Ch28:21 — "every willing man" — naming the person by the characteristic); *noun in construct* (2Ch29:31 — "willing heart"); *noun* (Psa51:12 — "willing spirit" — na.div as the characterological quality of the spirit). The grammatical range covers: adjective qualifying a constitutional location (heart/spirit), person-type noun, and abstract quality noun. The range reveals the characteristic operates as both a quality of inner faculties (willing heart/spirit) and as a defining person-type descriptor.

---

##### T7.1.3 — What is the semantic range of the primary term — across what breadth of meaning does it operate?

**[CHAR-1] Humility** (E)

The semantic range of the tapein- family and ka.na is broad. *Moral-relational:* the dominant register in this corpus — voluntary self-lowering before God (ka.na corpus); the character-disposition of the renewed person (tapeinofrosunē in Eph4:2; Phili2:3; Col3:12). *Social-circumstantial:* the humble estate of social lowness (tapeinōsis in Luk1:48; tapeinos in Luk1:52) — no explicit moral valuation but an objective social condition. *Creaturely-eschatological:* the lowly body (Phili3:21 tapeinōsis) — the creaturely condition of mortal embodiment. *Vocational:* Paul's ministry-humility (Act20:19 tapeinofrosunē) — sustained character in demanding contexts. The range spans social, moral, creaturely, and volitional registers — but the corpus concentrates in the moral-relational register.

**[CHAR-2] Submission** (E)

Hupakoē/hupakouō across the corpus span: *theological-soteriological:* obedience of faith (Rom1:5; 16:26 — the culminating response to the gospel); *Christological:* Christ's obedience as redemptive act (Rom5:19; Heb5:8); *communal-relational:* household obedience (Eph6:1; Col3:20); *apostolic-communal:* sustained community obedience to teaching (Phili2:12; 2Th3:14); *eschatological:* the peoples' submission to the coming ruler (Gen49:10); *volitional-inner:* heart-located obedience as the inner disposition (Rom6:17). The semantic range is unusually broad — from the most intimate inner disposition to cosmic eschatological telos — confirming the characteristic's structural centrality.

**[CHAR-3] Contrition** (E)

Dak.ka's semantic range within this corpus: *state of being crushed in spirit* (Psa34:18 — the inward crushing that produces the brokenhearted condition); *character of the spirit that is contrite* (Isa57:15 — the spirit characterised by this crushing, paired with lowliness). The semantic range in this corpus is deliberately focused: the term describes the inner state of the crushed/broken spirit and its character before God. It does not span the full semantic field of the Hebrew root (which can include physical crushing); within M09 the term is being used specifically for the inner-being register of spirit-crushing in relation to moral failure and divine holiness.

**[CHAR-4] Meekness and gentleness** (E)

Metriopatheō's semantic range in this corpus: *pastoral-mediating gentleness* toward the erring (Heb5:2 — dealing with the ignorant and wayward); this is the measured-compassionate-restraint register. Broader usage in Greek literature includes: measured emotional response that avoids both excessive passion and cold detachment (a Stoic virtue term). The term thus has both a general philosophical sense (emotional moderation) and a specifically relational-pastoral sense in Heb5:2 (compassionate measured dealing with the erring). Okneō's semantic range: hesitation, reluctance, slothful delay — in Act9:38 its absence is named as what is expected, so the semantic range is indirectly present as what the community expects not to find in Peter.

**[CHAR-5] Dignity** (E)

Semnotēs's semantic range across the three §3.D verses: *civic-public* (1Ti2:2 — the character quality enabling peaceful godly public life); *household-leadership* (1Ti3:4 — the inner gravity producing ordered household authority); *pedagogical* (Tit2:7 — the gravitas authenticating teaching). The full semantic range spans civic, familial, and pedagogical contexts — all three spheres of ordered human social existence. The range confirms the characteristic's comprehensive social-ordering function rather than its confinement to a single social sphere.

**[CHAR-6] Willing-heartedness** (E)

Na.div's semantic range in this corpus: *spontaneous inner generosity in worship-offering* (Exo35:5/22; 2Ch29:31 — free-will offering for tabernacle and temple); *wholehearted skilled service* (1Ch28:21 — willing man with skill for service); *spirit-level freely-given devotion after moral failure* (Psa51:12 — willing spirit as divine gift). The full range spans: corporate worship generosity, individual skilled service, and personal devotional orientation. The term thus operates across the communal, vocational, and personal registers — confirming it is a comprehensive characteristic rather than situationally confined.

---

##### T7.1.4 — Does the vocabulary include terms that distinguish distinct aspects of this characteristic — disposition versus act, received versus given, condition versus quality?

**[CHAR-1] Humility** (E)

Yes, the vocabulary distinguishes these aspects structurally. *Disposition:* tapeinofrosunē (the abstract noun naming the quality of character — Eph4:2; Act20:19). *Act:* tapeinoō/ka.na verbal (the event of humbling — Jam4:10; 2Ch7:14). *Condition:* tapeinōsis (the state noun — Luk1:48; Phili3:21). *Quality of person:* tapeinos (the adjective — Mat11:29). The distinction between disposition and act is especially clear: tapeinofrosunē names the stable character-quality; tapeinoō names the initiating volitional event. Both are present in the corpus and analytically distinguished, with Act20:19 providing the clearest bridge — Paul serving "with all humility" (tapeinofrosunē — disposition) "with tears and trials" (the contextual acts).

**[CHAR-2] Submission** (E)

Yes. *Act:* hupakouō verbal (Heb11:8 — obeyed; Act6:7 — became obedient); *Disposition:* hupakoē noun (Phili2:12 — "as you have always obeyed" — the characteristic as sustained inner orientation); *Condition:* hupotagē (Gal2:5 — "yield in submission" — the state of being ordered under); *Quality:* hupakoē as known characteristic (Rom16:19 — "your obedience is known to all"). The given-versus-received distinction is embedded in the relational structure: one obeys (gives submission) to an authority (who receives it). The vocabulary is structurally differentiated across these aspects.

**[CHAR-3] Contrition** (E)

Within the two-verse corpus, the vocabulary does not internally distinguish between disposition versus act, received versus given, or condition versus quality in the formal sense of separate terms. Both appearances of dak.ka are in the condition/quality register. However, Psa34:18's "brokenhearted" (nishbar-lev — shattered heart) adds a second term from the heart-level that together with dak.ka-ruach (crushed spirit) distinguishes the spirit-level manifestation from the heart-level manifestation of the same characteristic.

**[CHAR-4] Meekness and gentleness** (E)

Yes — the two terms distinguish two distinct aspects of the characteristic: metriopatheō names the *quality of relational engagement* (measured compassionate manner); okneō names the *kinetics of volitional readiness* (non-hesitating responsiveness). Together they cover the quality-of-engagement and the speed/freedom-of-response aspects. The distinction is genuine: one can have calibrated gentle engagement without instant responsiveness; one can have instant responsiveness without calibrated gentleness. The two terms together name the complete characteristic.

**[CHAR-5] Dignity** (S)

The three-verse corpus uses semnotēs exclusively — a single term in a single grammatical form. No vocabulary internal to the corpus distinguishes distinct aspects (disposition vs act, received vs given, condition vs quality). The single term names the quality consistently without aspect-differentiation. The distinction between the characteristic's inner quality and its social expression is present structurally (inner character → outward ordering) but is not marked by different vocabulary terms.

**[CHAR-6] Willing-heartedness** (E)

The single term na.div appears in both VCG contexts (VCG-01 — willing heart as self-generating free orientation; VCG-02 — willing spirit as divinely-given and sustained orientation after failure). The two VCG-level distinctions (spontaneous vs divinely-upheld) are marked by context and constitutional location (heart in VCG-01; spirit in VCG-02) rather than by different vocabulary terms. Within the term's usage, the constitutional location (heart/spirit) marks the distinction between the characteristic's two registers without a separate vocabulary term.

---

##### T7.1.5 — Does the vocabulary include a term for the structural opposite or absence of this characteristic?

**[CHAR-1] Humility** (E)

Yes, the vocabulary regularly pairs the humble terms with their structural opposites. *Greek:* hyperēphanos (arrogant, proud — Jam4:6; 1Pe5:5 — "God opposes the proud/hyperēphanos but gives grace to the humble"); hupsoō (to exalt/elevate — Mat23:12; Luk14:11 — "everyone who exalts himself will be humbled"). *Hebrew:* ga.on/gaavah (pride, arrogance — implicit in Pro11:2 — "when pride comes, then comes disgrace"). The structural opposite is consistently named and the contrast is structural to the characteristic's definition throughout the corpus.

**[CHAR-2] Submission** (E)

Yes. The corpus names *apeitheia* (disobedience) explicitly: Rom5:19 — "the one man's disobedience" as the structural counter to Christ's obedience; 2Th1:8 — "those who do not obey" — the verbal negative. Apeitheuō (to disobey) and apeitheia (disobedience) are the structural opposites named throughout the corpus. Pro30:17 — "scorns to obey" — names the dispositional opposite as contemptuous refusal. The structural opposite vocabulary is well-developed and consistently present as the relational counter to submission's yielding.

**[CHAR-3] Contrition** (S)

The two-verse corpus does not name the structural opposite of contrition directly. The structural opposite (hardened, proud, self-sufficient spirit) is implied by the contrast with those whom God does not describe as his dwelling partners (Isa57:15) but is not named with a specific term in these two verses.

**[CHAR-4] Meekness and gentleness** (E)

Yes. For metriopatheō: the structural opposites are implicit in the term — excessive severity (too much force/judgment toward the erring) or cold detachment (too little engagement). For okneō: the term itself names the opposite quality — oknos is hesitation, reluctance, slothful delay — the quality whose absence is the characteristic. The corpus thus contains both the structural opposite of okneō (in the term's own root) and the implied opposites of metriopatheō (severity and detachment).

**[CHAR-5] Dignity** (E)

The corpus does not name the structural opposite directly within the three verses. The structural opposite is M08 (Pride/Arrogance — proud self-display), but this is identified through the cross-register flag and the definitional analysis rather than through an explicit opposite term within §3.D. The absence of a named structural opposite within the corpus is consistent with the positive-quality focus of all three verses — they describe what semnotēs is rather than what it opposes.

**[CHAR-6] Willing-heartedness** (S)

The five-verse corpus does not directly name the structural opposite of na.div. The structural opposite (unwilling, reluctant, compelled) is present in the contrast structure (Exo35:5 — only the willing-hearted bring) but is not named with a specific opposing term in §3.E.

---

##### T7.1.6 — Does the vocabulary include a person-type term — a term for the one who habitually possesses or exercises this characteristic?

**[CHAR-1] Humility** (E)

Yes. *Tapeinos* as an adjective used substantively names the person-type: "gives grace to the humble" (Jam4:6; 1Pe5:5 — ho tapeinos); "he has looked on the humble estate" (Luk1:48 — tapeinōsis used of the person's condition). *Tsa.nu.a* (H6800B — Pro11:2 — "with the humble/tsa.nu.a is wisdom") is an adjectival form used to name the person-type in the Hebrew wisdom tradition. The person-type vocabulary is present in both testaments, confirming the characteristic as a stable personal quality capable of describing a type of person, not merely a momentary act.

**[CHAR-2] Submission** (E)

Yes. 1Pe1:14 — "as obedient children" (tekna hupakoēs — children of obedience) — the person-type is defined by the characteristic as a genitive relationship: the person whose defining identity is obedience to God. The adjectival use of hupakoē as a person-type marker confirms the characteristic can define a stable personal identity rather than merely describing occasional acts. Pro30:17 (by negation — the one who scorns to obey) also implies a person-type through the negative.

**[CHAR-3] Contrition** (E)

Yes. Psa34:18 — "the crushed in spirit" (dak.ka-ruach) — names the person characterised by the contrite state; "the brokenhearted" (nishbar-lev) — a second person-type term. Isa57:15 — "him who is of a contrite and lowly spirit" — the person-type characterised by the co-occurring states of contrition and lowliness. Both verses evidence person-type vocabulary, confirming that contrition can be a stable enough characterological state to name a type of person, not merely a momentary condition.

**[CHAR-4] Meekness and gentleness** (S)

The two-verse corpus does not include a person-type term (a term naming the one who habitually possesses meekness and gentleness). Heb5:2 addresses "he" (the high priest) as a specific role-holder rather than a generalised person-type; Act9:38 uses "him" (Peter) without a type term.

**[CHAR-5] Dignity** (S)

The three-verse corpus does not include a specific person-type term (a term naming the one who habitually has semnotēs). The verses address specific roles (the believer in 1Ti2:2, the overseer in 1Ti3:4, Titus in Tit2:7) rather than generalised person-type names.

**[CHAR-6] Willing-heartedness** (E)

Yes. 1Ch28:21 — "every willing man" (kol nadiv — every na.div person) — names the person-type defined by the characteristic. Exo35:22 — "all who were of a willing heart" — uses a construction that names a person-type by their characteristic. The person-type vocabulary is present across two of the five verses, confirming the characteristic can stably describe a type of person rather than only a momentary inner state.

---

##### T7.1.7 — Does the vocabulary include a supplication or seeking term — a term for the act of seeking this characteristic from another?

**[CHAR-1] Humility** (S)

The corpus does not directly evidence a distinct vocabulary term for seeking humility from another. The closest is the Phili4:12 "I have learned" (emathon — aorist of manthanō), suggesting the characteristic can be sought through learning and practice, but no specific supplication vocabulary targets humility as something sought from God or another person. This is a minor evidence gap — the characteristic is commanded and cultivated but seeking-vocabulary is not part of the attested terms.

**[CHAR-2] Submission** (S)

The corpus does not directly evidence a specific vocabulary term for seeking submission from another. The authority-relationship is constituted by the command-and-response structure (the authority commands; the person submits) rather than by a supplication register. This absence is analytically significant: submission is evoked by command rather than sought through supplication.

**[CHAR-3] Contrition** (S)

The two-verse corpus does not include a specific vocabulary term for seeking contrition from another. The characteristic is described as a state the person is in rather than something sought or asked for. Broader canonical awareness (e.g., Psa51:17 — "a broken and contrite heart, O God, you will not despise" — a nearby but outside-corpus verse) would add the seeking-of-acceptance dimension, but this is outside §3.B.

**[CHAR-4] Meekness and gentleness** (S)

The corpus does not include a vocabulary term for seeking meekness or gentleness from another.

**[CHAR-5] Dignity** (S)

The corpus does not include vocabulary for seeking semnotēs from another.

**[CHAR-6] Willing-heartedness** (E)

Yes — Psa51:12 — "uphold me with a willing spirit" — is a supplication for the characteristic: the psalmist seeks and asks for the willing spirit from God. The supplication vocabulary is present within the term's usage — one of the five verses is precisely the act of seeking the characteristic from God. This is distinctive: CHAR-6 is the only M09 characteristic in this bundle for which the corpus includes a direct supplication for the characteristic itself.

---

##### T7.1.8 — What does the relationship between the OT Hebrew vocabulary and the NT Greek vocabulary reveal about continuity or development of this characteristic across the Testaments?

**[CHAR-1] Humility** (E)

The relationship reveals *continuity of register with terminological development*. The OT Hebrew ka.na corpus establishes the register of voluntary humbling before God in response to divine address — the relational structure is fully formed in the OT. The NT Greek tapein- family does not contradict or replace this but *fills it out characterologically*: tapeinofrosunē (the character-disposition noun) appears predominantly in the Pauline corpus and is developed specifically as a virtue of the renewed community-life. The NT adds the Christological grounding (Phili2:8 — Christ's self-humbling as paradigm) that the OT anticipates typologically (T0.4.1) but does not state. The OT emphasises the act (ka.na verbal); the NT emphasises the disposition (tapeinofrosunē) and the character-quality (tapeinos). Together they present humility as an act that forms a disposition, with the NT providing the Christological grammar that makes the disposition intelligible.

**[CHAR-2] Submission** (E)

The OT Hebrew yiq.qe.hah (Gen49:10; Pro30:17) establishes the register of yielding allegiance to legitimate authority — in the eschatological promise (Gen49:10) and in wisdom-moral principle (Pro30:17). The NT Greek hupakoē/hupakouō/hupotagē family develops this register in three significant ways: (1) Christological grounding — Christ's own obedience (Heb5:8; Rom5:19) becomes the paradigm and the enabling ground; (2) Spirit-directed formation — 1Pe1:2 places the characteristic within the Spirit's sanctifying work; (3) gospel-telos — obedience of faith (Rom1:5; 16:26) frames submission as the inner response to the gospel's proclamation. The NT thus fills out the OT wisdom-moral register with Christological, pneumatological, and eschatological content.

**[CHAR-3] Contrition** (E)

The two-verse corpus is exclusively Hebrew OT with no NT Greek evidence for this characteristic directly within §3.B. This is analytically significant: contrition (dak.ka) as a named inner-being characteristic appears in the Hebrew wisdom and prophetic tradition but does not have a direct NT Greek terminological equivalent evidenced in this corpus. The Greek equivalent vocabulary (syntetrimenos — crushed, broken; katabrysis — grinding down) does not appear in the programme's M09 term universe as it was registered. This suggests either that contrition is absorbed into the tapein-/metanoia vocabulary in the NT (Greek humility/repentance terms carrying what Hebrew dak.ka carried) or that the characteristic's explicit naming in the NT requires a different term-set not registered in M09.

**[CHAR-4] Meekness and gentleness** (E)

The two-verse corpus is exclusively NT Greek — there is no Hebrew OT evidence for CHAR-4 within §3.C. This is analytically significant: metriopatheō and okneō are Greek terms without direct Hebrew OT equivalents in this corpus, suggesting the characteristic either: (a) has OT register in vocabulary not registered in M09 (e.g., Hebrew terms for gentleness like anaw/gentle or aph/slow to anger); or (b) receives particular development and explicit naming in the NT Greek context. The absence of Hebrew evidence limits the OT-NT relationship finding to an evidence gap rather than a comparative analysis.

**[CHAR-5] Dignity** (E)

The corpus is exclusively NT Greek — no Hebrew OT equivalent appears in this corpus. Semnotēs is a characteristically Hellenistic-Jewish virtue term that enters the Christian moral vocabulary through the Pastorals. Its absence from the Hebrew OT term universe in M09 suggests the characteristic's specifically social-ordering expression (civic, household, pedagogical gravity) receives its most explicit vocabulary in the NT Greek-cultural context, where the concept of dignified bearing (semnos) was a recognised virtue in Hellenistic moral philosophy. The OT tradition carries related qualities (e.g., wisdom, reverence-worthiness) but not under this specific term in the programme's registry.

**[CHAR-6] Willing-heartedness** (E)

The five-verse corpus is exclusively OT Hebrew — na.div/nadab does not appear in the NT Greek evidence of M09. This is analytically significant: the characteristic's most direct vocabulary is OT Hebrew, without direct NT Greek equivalent in this corpus. The NT may carry the register through different vocabulary (prothymia — readiness, eagerness — 2Cor8:11/12 — outside corpus; or the broader Spirit-given willing-devotion language), but within §3.E the evidence is exclusively OT. The absence of NT Greek evidence means the OT-NT continuity finding is limited to the typological resonance (T0.4.1) rather than terminological continuity.

---

##### T7.1.9 — Is there a term newly coined in the NT period for this characteristic — and if so, what does that coinage reveal?

**[CHAR-1] Humility** (E)

Tapeinofrosunē (G5012) is widely considered a NT coinage or at least a term rare before the NT period that becomes prominent in the Pauline and early Christian vocabulary. Its coinage as a *compound noun* (tapeinos + phronēo — low-minded, humble-thinking) reveals that the early Christian community needed a term that named the *disposition of mind and character* rather than merely the act or the condition. The coinage of tapeinofrosunē signals the development of humility from an occasional covenantal act (ka.na register) and an adverse social condition into a positive character virtue — a development the science extract confirms is counter-cultural (the Greco-Roman world did not prize humility as a virtue). The Christological grounding (Phili2:5–8) provides the reason for the positive valorisation: the divine Son took on the tapeinōsis register voluntarily, making it a virtue rather than merely a deficit.

**[CHAR-2] Submission** (E)

Hupotagē (G5292 — noun of condition: being ordered under) is less common before the NT period and is particularly associated with the Pauline corpus. Its coinage or prominent use in Paul's letters (Gal2:5; 2Cor9:13; 1Ti2:11; 1Ti3:4) reflects the need to name the inner condition of being in ordered submission — not merely the act (hupakouō) or the quality (hupakoē) but the structural positioning itself. The coinage reveals that the early Christian community needed vocabulary to name the *condition* of being rightly ordered under legitimate authority within the community structures (household, church, apostolic teaching), not just the act of obeying.

**[CHAR-3] Contrition** (S)

The two-verse corpus is exclusively OT Hebrew; no NT coinage evidence is present in §3.B. The NT development of the contrition register (if any) is outside this corpus.

**[CHAR-4] Meekness and gentleness** (E)

Metriopatheō appears to be relatively rare in pre-NT Greek literature and receives specific theological-ethical development in the NT context. Its use in Heb5:2 is one of only a handful of NT occurrences. The term's application to the high priest's priestly character represents a specific theological deployment of a philosophical virtue term (measured affective response) in a distinctly Christological-priestly context. The coinage (or rare term's deployment) reveals that the early Christian/Jewish-Christian tradition needed a term that expressed calibrated compassionate engagement as a priestly quality — the measured-affect term from philosophical ethics is redeployed in a theological-pastoral frame.

**[CHAR-5] Dignity** (E)

Semnotēs as a *virtue term in this specific sense* (stable moral gravity as a Christian character qualification) receives its distinctive Christian deployment in the Pastorals. While semnos/semnotēs existed in classical and Hellenistic Greek (indicating venerable, august character), its use as a specific Christian virtue in the context of community leadership and teaching (1Ti2:2; 1Ti3:4; Tit2:7) represents a distinctive theological appropriation of the Hellenistic virtue term — reframing semnos/semnotēs as a specifically Christian character quality grounded in godliness (eusebeia) and integrity (Tit2:7) rather than in social status or philosophical stoic virtue.

**[CHAR-6] Willing-heartedness** (S)

The five-verse corpus is exclusively OT Hebrew; no NT coinage evidence is present in §3.E. The NT development of the willing-heartedness register, if any, would use different vocabulary not registered in M09's na.div term.

---

##### T7.1.10 — What does the full vocabulary arc reveal about this characteristic's complete semantic range?

**[CHAR-1] Humility** (E)

The full vocabulary arc (ka.na → tsa.na/tsa.nu.a → tapeinos → tapeinofrosunē → tapeinoō → tapeinōsis) reveals a characteristic that spans: *act* (ka.na verbal, tapeinoō), *disposition* (tapeinofrosunē, tsa.nu.a as adjective), *condition* (tapeinōsis, tsa.na as state), *person-quality* (tapeinos as adjective). The semantic range covers *voluntary and imposed*, *inner-act and outer-condition*, *OT-covenantal and NT-Christological*, *wisdom-epistemic and community-relational*, and *present-dispositional and eschatological-anticipatory* registers. The arc is comprehensive — the characteristic operates across every register of the inner being's posture before God and others, which confirms its foundational role in the inner-being landscape (T6.1.2 — humility as meta-characteristic enabling the operation of multiple other characteristics).

**[CHAR-2] Submission** (E)

The full vocabulary arc (yiq.qe.hah → hupakoē → hupakouō → hupotagē) reveals a characteristic that spans: *act* (obeying), *quality* (obedience as inner disposition), *condition* (being ordered under), *person-type* (obedient children). The semantic range covers eschatological (Gen49:10), wisdom-moral (Pro30:17), Christological (Heb5:8; Rom5:19), gospel-responsive (Rom1:5; Act6:7), household-formational (Eph6:1; 1Ti3:4), and soul-purifying (1Pe1:22) registers. The arc confirms the characteristic's central and wide-ranging role in the inner-being landscape — it is not a peripheral virtue but a structural one that appears wherever the person is in relation to legitimate authority, from the most immediate household to the cosmic-eschatological.

**[CHAR-3] Contrition** (E)

The vocabulary arc is confined to two appearances of dak.ka across two verses. The arc reveals: *person-type* (crushed in spirit, brokenhearted — Psa34:18; contrite and lowly spirit — Isa57:15); *spirit-level location* (both verses); *heart-level location* (Psa34:18 — brokenhearted). The arc is deliberately narrow — one term, two appearances — which confirms the characteristic's thin evidentiary base and the need for cross-canonical investigation to complete the vocabulary portrait. The absence of NT Greek evidence suggests the characteristic's fuller articulation may require tracking the Hebrew register across the full OT canon and its NT receptions in repentance and brokenness vocabulary.

**[CHAR-4] Meekness and gentleness** (E)

The vocabulary arc (metriopatheō + okneō) reveals a characteristic constituted by two complementary terms: one naming the quality of engagement (measured-within-bounds compassion) and one naming the quality of will (non-hesitating readiness). The arc is narrow (two terms, two appearances) but the two terms together cover a distinctive inner-being profile: the person who engages gently with the erring and responds promptly without self-protective delay is comprehensively described by this pair. The absence of Hebrew OT terms in the corpus is the most significant evidence gap in the vocabulary arc — it limits the OT-NT comparison and suggests CHAR-4 may need expanded registry coverage to complete the vocabulary portrait.

**[CHAR-5] Dignity** (E)

The vocabulary arc is narrow (one term, three appearances) but analytically significant: semnotēs across three contexts (civic, household, pedagogical) reveals a characteristic with comprehensive social-ordering reach. The arc confirms: the characteristic is a *noun-of-quality* (stable inner character property); it operates across the full range of the believer's social existence ("in every way" — 1Ti2:2); it has no internal vocabulary differentiation (single term for all aspects). The absence of Hebrew OT vocabulary is the most significant gap — the characteristic's OT antecedents (if any) are not registered in M09 and would require expanded registry work.

**[CHAR-6] Willing-heartedness** (E)

The vocabulary arc (five appearances of na.div across three OT books — Exodus, Chronicles, Psalms) reveals a characteristic with consistent meaning across historical, narrative, and lyrical contexts: (a) *person-type* (1Ch28:21 — every willing man); (b) *quality of constitutional location* (Exo35:5/22; 2Ch29:31 — willing heart; Psa51:12 — willing spirit); (c) *supplication* (Psa51:12 — seeking the willing spirit from God). The arc spans tabernacle-construction (Exo35), temple-service preparation (1Ch28:21), temple-restoration worship (2Ch29:31), and personal penitential prayer (Psa51:12). The OT-only evidence base is the most significant limitation of the arc — the NT development of this register is not represented in the programme's na.div corpus.

---

#### T7.2 — Verse and Literary Interpretation

##### T7.2.1 — What is the function of this characteristic's primary term within its primary verse — what role does it play in the sentence and argument?

**[CHAR-1] Humility** (E)

The primary anchor verse of the characteristic, based on the Phase 7 designation, is 2Ch7:14 (vc_id 60903 — anchor of M09-A-VCG-01). The function of ka.na ("humble themselves") in the sentence: it is the first in a conditional sequence — "if my people… humble themselves, and pray and seek my face and turn from their wicked ways, then I will hear." Ka.na is the syntactically first and logically foundational act in the conditional clause: it initiates the series of acts that together constitute the full movement of return to God. Without the humbling, the subsequent acts (prayer, seeking, turning) do not have their inner-being foundation. The term functions as the *initiating condition* of the conditional structure — the first inner act without which the promised divine response ("I will hear, forgive, heal") cannot proceed.

**[CHAR-2] Submission** (E)

The Phase 7 anchor of M09-C-VCG-01 is vc_id 61397 (Rom6:17 — "obedient from the heart"). In this verse, hupakouō (implied by "obedient") functions as the *predicate complement* — it defines what the Roman believers have become through the transformation from sin's slavery: they have become "obedient from the heart." The term's function is to name the constitutional quality that the transformation has produced — not an act being described but a character-state that now defines the person. The phrase "from the heart" is the qualifying locative that distinguishes this genuine submission from mere compliance — placing it constitutionally in the inner person rather than at the behavioural surface.

**[CHAR-3] Contrition** (E)

The Phase 7 anchor of M09-E-VCG-01 is vc_id 62969 (Isa57:15). In this verse, dak.ka functions as an *adjectival qualifier of spirit* within a relative clause: "with him who is of a contrite [dak.ka] and lowly spirit." The term qualifies the spirit, making the condition of the spirit the criteria for God's dwelling. The function is constitutive: dak.ka names the specific inner-being condition that qualifies the person for God's dwelling-with. In the sentence's argument, dak.ka is the defining inner-being criterion paired with lowly (shpal) that specifies who receives the divine indwelling alongside the divine transcendent dwelling. Without dak.ka, the criteria for God's relational nearness would be incomplete.

**[CHAR-4] Meekness and gentleness** (E)

The Phase 7 anchor of M09-F-VCG-01 is vc_id 6225 (Heb5:2). Metriopatheō functions as an *infinitive complement* to the modal verb "can" (dunatai): "he can deal gently" — naming the capacity that the high priest possesses and deploys in his mediating role. The function in the argument is causal-enabling: the high priest's metriopatheō capacity is grounded in his shared weakness, making him fit for his priestly function. The term is the key to the argument's claim about priestly fitness — without the capacity named by metriopatheō, the high priest would be constitutionally unfit for his role. The term thus functions as the name of the priestly qualification.

**[CHAR-5] Dignity** (E)

The Phase 7 anchor of M09-G-VCG-01 is vc_id 12081 (1Ti2:2). Semnotēs functions as part of a *compound descriptor of the desired manner of life*: "godly and dignified in every way" — the adverbial phrase "in every way" gives the comprehensive scope, while semnotēs names one of the two qualities (alongside godliness) that characterise this life. The function is *characterological qualification* of the desired manner of Christian existence in the civic sphere — semnotēs names the manner-quality alongside the content-quality (godliness). In the argument (prayer for rulers enabling a peaceful quiet life), semnotēs is the manner-quality that makes such a life genuinely honourable rather than merely comfortable.

**[CHAR-6] Willing-heartedness** (E)

The Phase 7 anchor of M09-H-VCG-01 is vc_id 43495 (Exo35:5). Na.div (as nadiv — willing, generous) functions as an *adjectival qualifier of lev (heart)* within a conditional relative clause: "whoever is of a generous/willing heart [lev nadiv], let him bring." The function is *defining the inner condition for the outward act* — the na.div quality of the heart is the constitutional criterion for the bringing. In the argument (Moses instructing the community on contributions for the tabernacle), the term functions as the discriminating criterion: not all are to bring equally, but only those whose hearts are genuinely willing — the inner condition defines who the appropriate giver is. The term thus identifies the character-type (willing-hearted person) whose offering is the kind God seeks.

---

##### T7.2.2 — What literary form carries the primary verse evidence (narrative, psalm, wisdom, prophecy, epistle, apocalyptic) — and what does that form require for responsible interpretation?

**[CHAR-1] Humility** (E)

The Humility evidence spans multiple literary forms. The dominant form in M09-A-VCG-01 (ka.na corpus) is *historical narrative* (Kings and Chronicles). Responsible interpretation requires: attending to the narrative's evaluative purpose (the Chronicler uses the ka.na pattern as a theological explanation of historical outcomes — judgment averted or applied corresponds to the presence or absence of humbling); recognising that the narrative presents normative theological patterns, not merely descriptive history. The NT evidence spans *epistle* (Phili2; Eph4; Col3; Jam4; 1Pe5) and *synoptic teaching* (Mat11:29; Mat18:4; Mat23:12; Luk14:11; Luk18:14). Epistolary humility texts require attention to their communal-paraenetic function: they are shaping community life, not abstractly defining virtue. Synoptic texts require attention to their kingdom-reversal logic: the exaltation-of-the-humble pattern is part of the eschatological inversion of values in Jesus's teaching.

**[CHAR-2] Submission** (E)

The dominant literary form is *epistle*, specifically Pauline epistle and the Petrine epistles. The household codes (Eph6; Col3) are paraenetic-communal in character, shaping the community's relational patterns. The Romans material (6:12–17) is theological argument — establishing the anthropological basis of the submission-to-righteousness narrative. Heb5:8 is theological exposition of Christological significance. Gen49:10 is patriarchal-prophetic poetry (the Blessing of Jacob). Pro30:17 is Hebrew wisdom. The epistolary form requires attending to the communal-formational function: the epistles are shaping community life rather than abstractly defining virtue. The wisdom form (Pro30:17) requires attending to the universal-principle claim: filial obedience as a wisdom insight applicable beyond specific contexts.

**[CHAR-3] Contrition** (E)

Psa34:18 is *Psalm* — specifically a wisdom-acrostic psalm (Psa34 is an alphabetic acrostic). The psalm form requires attending to its generalising character: the verse makes a consistent characterological statement about God's relation to the brokenhearted, not a statement about a specific individual's experience. Isa57:15 is *prophetic discourse* — divine first-person speech within Isaiah's oracles. Prophetic discourse requires attending to its authoritative divine-voice character: the verse is God's own self-description of where he dwells. The combination of psalm-generalisation and prophetic-divine-speech gives the two-verse corpus a double authority: both the wisdom tradition and the prophetic tradition converge on the same finding about God's relation to the contrite.

**[CHAR-4] Meekness and gentleness** (E)

Heb5:2 is *theological epistle* — specifically the sustained theological argument of Hebrews about the nature of priesthood and the superiority of Christ's priesthood. The literary form requires attending to: (a) the argument's cumulative logic (Heb5:2 is part of a chain of reasoning leading to Christ as superior high priest — Heb4:14–5:10); (b) the typological framework (Aaronic priesthood as type of Christ's); (c) the epistle's rhetorical purpose of grounding the audience's faith in Christ's superior priestly mediation. Act9:38 is *narrative* (Acts narrative of early church history) — requiring attention to the scene's details as a concrete instance of the expected quality of apostolic responsiveness.

**[CHAR-5] Dignity** (E)

All three verses are within *pastoral epistle* — the Pastoral Letters (1 Timothy, Titus) as a sub-genre of Pauline epistle addressed to community leaders for the ordering of community life. The literary form requires attending to: (a) the paraenetic purpose — these are formational directives for community character, not theological argument; (b) the leadership-qualification function of 1Ti3:4 and Tit2:7 — within formal lists of leadership qualifications, the terms carry normative weight; (c) the comprehensive scope of 1Ti2:2 — the prayer-context gives semnotēs the frame of the whole desired Christian existence, not just leadership.

**[CHAR-6] Willing-heartedness** (E)

Exo35:5 and Exo35:22 are *narrative* (Pentateuchal narrative of tabernacle-construction). The narrative form requires attending to the historical-redemptive significance of the tabernacle as the site of God's dwelling among Israel, and to the community's response as normative for understanding willing-heartedness in the worship-offering register. 1Ch28:21 is *narrative* (Chronicler's history — speech within a historical narrative). 2Ch29:31 is *narrative* (Chronicler's account of Hezekiah's reform). Psa51:12 is *psalm* — specifically the great penitential psalm of the Psalter. The psalm form requires attending to its liturgical and instructional function: Psa51 is a formative prayer for the community's use in penitential situations, making the willing-spirit prayer normative for anyone in the situation of moral failure seeking restoration.

---

##### T7.2.3 — What is the logical structure of key arguments in the verse evidence — what are the premises and conclusions?

**[CHAR-1] Humility** (E)

Key logical structures: *2Ch7:14:* Premise 1 — God's people are called by his name (covenantal standing). Premise 2 — They are to humble themselves, pray, seek, and turn. Conclusion — God will hear, forgive, and heal. The argument is conditional-covenantal. *Phili2:3–8:* Premise 1 — The community is to do nothing from selfish ambition. Premise 2 — In humility each is to count others more significant. Premise 3 — Christ humbled himself to the point of death in obedience to the Father. Conclusion (implicit) — the community's humility participates in the pattern of the Son. *Jam4:6–10:* Premise 1 — God gives more grace. Premise 2 — God opposes the proud but gives grace to the humble. Conclusion — Submit to God; humble yourselves before the Lord; and he will exalt you. The argument is wisdom-principle → covenantal application → eschatological promise.

**[CHAR-2] Submission** (E)

Key logical structures: *Rom6:16–17:* Premise 1 — you present yourselves to whoever you obey as slaves. Premise 2 — you were slaves to sin; you have become obedient from the heart to the teaching. Conclusion: your master has changed; you are now slaves of righteousness (transitional premise → conclusion). *1Pe1:22:* Premise — you have purified your souls by obedience to the truth. Conclusion — love one another earnestly from a pure heart (purification → affective-fruit). *Heb5:8–9:* Premise — the Son learned obedience through suffering. Conclusion — being made perfect, he became the source of eternal salvation to all who obey him (formation → salvific enablement).

**[CHAR-3] Contrition** (E)

*Psa34:18:* Premise — the Lord is near to the brokenhearted and saves the crushed in spirit. This is a generalising wisdom-principle: the Lord's characterological disposition is nearness to the broken. The logical structure is a simple divine-character statement with an implied promise-application. *Isa57:15:* Premise 1 — I (God) dwell in the high and holy place. Premise 2 — I also dwell with him who is of a contrite and lowly spirit. Premise 3 — in order to revive the spirit of the lowly and the heart of the contrite. Conclusion (implied) — therefore the contrite person is where God's reviving is found. The logical structure is a divine self-description of double dwelling (transcendent and immanent) with the purpose of the immanent dwelling explicitly named.

**[CHAR-4] Meekness and gentleness** (E)

*Heb5:2:* Premise 1 — the high priest is appointed to act on behalf of humans before God (v.1 — implied). Premise 2 — he can deal gently with the ignorant and wayward. Premise 3 — he himself is beset with weakness. Conclusion: his shared weakness is the ground that enables his measured-compassionate function. The argument structure is: shared-weakness-as-enabling-ground → calibrated-priestly-capacity. This is a surprising inversion of the expected logic (weakness → capacity rather than strength → capacity), which is the key rhetorical move. *Act9:38:* implicit argument: urgent genuine need → expected response is immediate presence without hesitation.

**[CHAR-5] Dignity** (E)

*1Ti2:2:* Premise — prayer for rulers enables peaceful public existence. Premise — peaceful existence enables godly, dignified life. Conclusion (implied) — the dignified manner of life is the content of the quiet existence for which prayer is made. The argument confirms semnotēs as the character-quality of ordered, godly social existence rather than of mere passive quietude. *1Ti3:4:* Premise — the overseer must manage his household well. Premise — managing well includes maintaining dignity and producing submissive children. Conclusion — dignity and produced-submission are the tests of effective household leadership. *Tit2:7:* Premise — the teacher must be a model of good works. Premise — in teaching, integrity and dignity are required qualities. Conclusion — these qualities are the character-authentication of genuine teaching.

**[CHAR-6] Willing-heartedness** (E)

*Exo35:5:* Premise — the Lord's contribution is needed for the tabernacle. Premise — God calls for it from those of willing heart. Conclusion — the appropriate giver is the one whose inner condition is genuinely free. The argument identifies inner condition as the discriminating criterion for genuine worship-contribution. *Psa51:12:* Premise — God restores the joy of salvation (the preceding plea). Premise — the psalmist needs a willing spirit to sustain freely-offered devotion. Conclusion — seek God to uphold the willing spirit. The argument positions the willing spirit as both the necessary condition for ongoing devotion and a gift requiring divine sustaining.

---

##### T7.2.4 — What contextual setting carries the primary verse evidence (judicial, liturgical, covenantal, communal, eschatological) — and what does that setting reveal?

**[CHAR-1] Humility** (E)

The primary contextual settings are: *Covenantal-judicial* (the ka.na corpus — Kings and Chronicles): the setting is Israel's covenant relationship and its judicial consequences. Humility is the inner act required within the covenant when the people have broken its terms. *Community-formational* (Phili2; Eph4; Col3): the setting is the early Christian community being shaped into a body of believers. Humility is the foundational disposition for community life. *Kingdom-eschatological* (Mat18:4; Mat23:12; Luk18:14): the setting is Jesus's teaching on the kingdom of God. Humility is the defining inner posture of the kingdom-citizen. *Personal-devotional* (Act20:19 — Paul's ministry memoir; Phili4:12 — Paul's learned contentment): the setting is personal vocation and its formation over time. Each setting reveals a different dimension of the characteristic: covenantal, communal, kingdom, and vocational.

**[CHAR-2] Submission** (E)

Multiple contextual settings: *Theological-anthropological* (Romans 6 — the transformation from sin's slavery to righteousness through heart-obedience; the defining narrative of the Christian's inner constitution). *Community-formational* (Phili2:12; 2Th3:14; Phile21 — the community's obedience shaped and maintained through apostolic relationship). *Household* (Eph6:1; Col3:20; 1Ti3:4 — obedience within family authority structures). *Liturgical-covenantal* (Gen49:10 — patriarchal blessing setting; 1Pe1:2 — the trinitarian framing of the believer's calling). *Eschatological* (2Th1:8 — the judgment on non-obedience; Gen49:10 — the future gathering). Each setting reveals a dimension: the theological-anthropological setting reveals the deepest inner stakes; the community setting reveals the relational expression; the household setting reveals the formation context; the eschatological setting reveals the ultimate scope.

**[CHAR-3] Contrition** (E)

Psa34:18's setting is *liturgical-wisdom* — the psalm as a form of communal prayer and instruction. The setting implies the verse is a formative teaching-truth for the worshipping community rather than a narrative report of one event. Isa57:15's setting is *prophetic-covenantal* — divine address to Israel in the context of Isaiah's extended oracle about restoration and the nature of the God who restores. The setting implies a word of comfort and promise to a community in exile or spiritual dissolution: the holy God will dwell with the broken among them.

**[CHAR-4] Meekness and gentleness** (E)

Heb5:2's setting is *theological-priestly*: the Hebrews argument about the nature of adequate priestly mediation between God and erring humanity. The setting reveals that the characteristic is not a general virtue but a specifically *mediating quality* — it is the quality that makes the mediator adequate to their role. Act9:38's setting is *pastoral-apostolic*: the early church's network of need and response, where the apostle's prompt responsive presence is essential to ministry.

**[CHAR-5] Dignity** (E)

Three distinct settings: *Civic-liturgical* (1Ti2:2 — the prayer-for-rulers context situates semnotēs in the believer's civic existence under governance); *Household-leadership qualification* (1Ti3:4 — formal qualification list for overseer appointment); *Pedagogical-character standard* (Tit2:7 — Titus as model teacher). Each setting reveals a different dimension: the civic setting confirms the characteristic's public-existence scope; the qualification setting confirms it as a measurable character standard; the pedagogical setting confirms it as a teaching-authenticating quality.

**[CHAR-6] Willing-heartedness** (E)

Three distinct settings: *Tabernacle-construction* (Exo35:5/22 — the wilderness community constructing God's dwelling place; the free-will offering as the community's response to divine dwelling); *Temple-service preparation* (1Ch28:21 — David's preparation for Solomon's temple-building; skilled willing service for God's permanent dwelling); *Temple-restoration worship* (2Ch29:31 — Hezekiah's reform restoring proper worship after apostasy; willing-hearted offering as the sign of renewed covenant devotion); *Penitential personal prayer* (Psa51:12 — the individual before God after moral failure; the willing spirit as the condition for renewed devotion). The settings range from communal-institutional to intensely personal.

---

##### T7.2.5 — Does any verse function as the primary anchor for this characteristic — the verse that most fully and directly expresses its essential character?

**[CHAR-1] Humility** (E)

Two verses compete for primary anchor status. *2Ch7:14* (ka.na — the covenantal-conditional sequence) — the most comprehensive single verse stating the inner act's covenantal structure and divine consequence. *Phili2:3* with its Christological context in Phili2:8 — the NT definition of humility as counting others more significant, grounded in Christ's own self-humbling as paradigm. *Mat11:29* — "gentle and lowly in heart" — the most explicit heart-location of the characteristic in Jesus's own self-description. The Phase 7 designation (anchor of M09-A-VCG-01 = 2Ch7:14) provides the formal anchor; but analytically, Mat11:29 (the only verse that explicitly locates the characteristic "in the heart" while attributing it to Christ as self-description) has the strongest claim as the definitionally richest anchor for the full characteristic at CHAR-1 scope.

**[CHAR-2] Submission** (E)

Two verses compete. *Rom6:17* (phase 7 designated anchor of M09-C-VCG-01) — "obedient from the heart to the standard of teaching" — the most direct statement of submission as a genuine transformation of the inner person's core disposition, distinguished from external compliance by the heart-location. *Heb5:8* (anchor of M09-C-VCG-04) — "he learned obedience through what he suffered" — the Christological paradigm that frames the characteristic as something acquired through costly formation rather than naturally possessed. Both are definitive; analytically, Rom6:17 is the most directly inner-being statement (obedience from the heart as core disposition); Heb5:8 is the most Christologically paradigmatic.

**[CHAR-3] Contrition** (E)

Isa57:15 (vc_id 62969) is the more analytically comprehensive of the two verses and functions as the primary anchor (designated as such at Phase 7): it names both the characteristic (contrite) and its companion (lowly), names both constitutional locations (spirit-level — "revive the spirit"; heart-level — "revive the heart"), and provides God's explicit statement of his own dwelling disposition toward the contrite. No other verse in the corpus provides this comprehensive a statement.

**[CHAR-4] Meekness and gentleness** (E)

Heb5:2 (vc_id 6225) is the primary anchor (designated at Phase 7). It provides the most analytically comprehensive statement of the characteristic: naming the quality (metriopatheō), naming the objects of the quality (the ignorant and wayward), naming the enabling ground (shared weakness), and framing it within the priestly-mediating context that gives the characteristic its theological significance.

**[CHAR-5] Dignity** (E)

1Ti2:2 (vc_id 12081) is the primary anchor (designated at Phase 7): it provides the most encompassing statement of semnotēs — paired with godliness, framed within a prayer for peaceful existence, and qualified as "in every way" — establishing the characteristic's comprehensive scope, its pairing with the most fundamental orientation of Christian existence (godliness), and its function in the civic-public sphere. The other two verses are more specific (leadership qualification, teaching model).

**[CHAR-6] Willing-heartedness** (E)

Two verses serve as anchors for the two VCGs: vc_id 43495 (Exo35:5) anchors M09-H-VCG-01; vc_id 43494 (Psa51:12) anchors M09-H-VCG-02. Analytically, Exo35:5 is the most definitional for the characteristic's primary positive form (the willing heart as the inner condition that produces genuine worship-contribution); Psa51:12 is the most distinctive for the characteristic's restoration dimension (the willing spirit as divine gift sought after failure).

---

##### T7.2.6 — What does the primary anchor verse reveal that no other verse reveals?

**[CHAR-1] Humility** (E)

Mat11:29 ("gentle and lowly in heart") reveals two things no other single verse in the corpus reveals: (1) the explicit *heart-location* of the characteristic as a self-description of Jesus — no other verse both locates it "in the heart" and attributes it to the divine-human person in self-description; (2) the direct *invitation to learn the characteristic from Christ himself* as the teacher — "learn from me" positions Christ not merely as exemplar to be observed but as teacher to be apprenticed to. 2Ch7:14 reveals the covenantal structure and divine promise; Phili2:8 reveals the Christological paradigm. Only Mat11:29 reveals that the characteristic is simultaneously located in the heart, possessed by Jesus as his own self-description, and the subject of personal invitation to learn — making it the most direct entry-point into the characteristic's essential nature as the inner posture of the person who has learned from Christ.

**[CHAR-2] Submission** (E)

Rom6:17 reveals uniquely: the combination of (1) *heart-location* ("from the heart" — the most explicit single-verse heart-location of genuine submission), (2) *transformational character* ("you who were once slaves of sin have become obedient" — the past-to-present transformation), (3) *content-specificity* ("to the standard of teaching to which you were committed" — submission is not formless but takes a doctrinal content as its object), and (4) *thanking God* ("thanks be to God" — the framing of this transformation as divine gift). No other verse combines these four dimensions in one statement. The verse is the most comprehensive single-verse expression of submission as a constitutionally located, divine-gift transformation of the inner person's core orientation.

**[CHAR-3] Contrition** (E)

Isa57:15 reveals uniquely: (1) *God's explicit self-description of double dwelling* — he inhabits eternity-holiness and the contrite spirit simultaneously, placing contrition as the inner condition most aligned with divine self-disclosure; (2) *the purpose of dwelling* — "to revive the spirit of the lowly, and to revive the heart of the contrite" — the reviving is the explicit telos of God's dwelling with the contrite; (3) *the pairing of contrition with lowliness* in a single phrase that distinguishes and co-defines the two characteristics. No other verse combines these three elements: the divine-holiness framing, the explicit purpose of reviving, and the definitional pairing with lowliness.

**[CHAR-4] Meekness and gentleness** (E)

Heb5:2 reveals uniquely: (1) the *enabling ground* of the characteristic — shared creaturely weakness as the constitutional basis for metriopatheō; (2) the *priestly-mediating context* — the characteristic as a quality specifically suited to mediating between God and the erring; (3) the *inversion logic* — weakness as enabling capacity rather than disabling limitation. No other verse in §3.C provides these three dimensions. The verse is uniquely the window into how metriopatheō is constitutionally produced (through shared weakness) and why it is theologically significant (as priestly mediation quality).

**[CHAR-5] Dignity** (E)

1Ti2:2 reveals uniquely: (1) the *comprehensive scope* of semnotēs — "in every way" places the characteristic as the manner-quality of the whole of Christian existence, not just specific roles; (2) the *civic-public dimension* — the prayer-for-governance context places the characteristic in the believer's relation to the public civic sphere; (3) the *godliness-pairing* that confirms the characteristic is the social-manner expression of God-directed life rather than a self-standing social virtue. No other verse in the corpus provides all three dimensions together.

**[CHAR-6] Willing-heartedness** (E)

Exo35:5 (VCG-01 anchor) reveals uniquely: the *discriminating criterion function* of the characteristic — "whoever is of a willing heart, let him bring" — the willing-heartedness is explicitly what discriminates appropriate from inappropriate giving; it is the inner condition that makes the offering genuine rather than compelled. No other verse in the corpus makes this discriminating function as explicit. Psa51:12 (VCG-02 anchor) reveals uniquely: the characteristic as *divine gift requiring supplication after failure* — no other verse in the corpus directly names the seeking of willing-heartedness from God; Psa51:12 is the only verse where the characteristic must be sought and upheld by divine action rather than naturally present.

---

#### T7.3 — Human Science Frameworks

##### T7.3.1 — Which human science framework (psychology, moral philosophy, developmental psychology, sociology, anthropology, or other) is most useful as an interpretive lens for this characteristic?

**[CHAR-1] Humility** (E)

Per the science extract (wa-m09-humility-scienceextract-v1_0-20260513.md), the most useful primary framework is *positive psychology / moral psychology* (Section 1 of the extract). Tangney's framework distinguishing genuine humility from self-abasement (accurate self-appraisal, openness to new information, ability to acknowledge limitations without excessive self-focus) maps closely onto the corpus's distinction between genuine tapeinofrosunē and the false humility of Col2:18/23. Davis and colleagues' distinction between other-focused humility (orientation toward others) and self-focused humility (accurate self-knowledge) maps directly onto the corpus's two primary registers: Phili2:3 (other-focused — counting others more significant) and the ka.na self-assessment pattern (accurate self-knowledge before God).

**[CHAR-2] Submission** (E)

Per the science extract (wa-m09-humility-scienceextract-v1_0-20260513.md), the most useful framework is *social psychology / organisational psychology* (Section 3 — Submission, obedience, and deference). The Milgram obedience research, the organisational psychology distinction between compliant and internalised obedience, and Cialdini's compliance research provide the primary analytical lens. Critically, the organisational psychology distinction between *compliant obedience* (behavioural compliance without inner agreement) and *internalised obedience* (compliance rooted in genuine agreement with the authority's values) maps directly onto the M09-C/D split: M09-C is the internalised-obedience register; M09-D is the relational-pattern expression that may or may not be backed by M09-C inner disposition.

**[CHAR-3] Contrition** (E)

Per the science extract (wa-m09-humility-scienceextract-v1_0-20260513.md), the most relevant section is §1 (Humility as psychological trait and virtue), specifically the discussion of contrition: "Contriteness (dak.ka — crushed, broken in spirit) represents the acute form of humility produced by genuine confrontation with one's failure." Clinical psychology and moral psychology provide the primary lens: specifically the psychology of guilt, moral repair, and the phenomenology of genuine remorse (as distinct from shame, which is more self-focused). The distinction between *guilt* (moral self-assessment of specific actions) and *shame* (global self-condemnation) is analytically important here.

**[CHAR-4] Meekness and gentleness** (E)

Per the science extract (wa-m09-humility-scienceextract-v1_0-20260513.md), the most relevant section is §2 (Meekness, gentleness, and self-restraint). The primary framework is *clinical psychology / virtue psychology*: specifically self-regulation research (Baumeister and Vohs — self-control as the ability to override dominant impulse responses) and virtue psychology (Peterson and Seligman's VIA framework — meekness as controlled strength, a stable disposition). The Heb5:2 inversion logic (weakness enabling capacity) is particularly illuminated by psychological research on empathy and compassion: the capacity for genuine empathy is enhanced by genuine acquaintance with one's own vulnerability.

**[CHAR-5] Dignity** (E)

Per the science extract (wa-m09-humility-scienceextract-v1_0-20260513.md), the most relevant section is §2 (Meekness, gentleness, and self-restraint), specifically the dignity studies component: "Dignity (semnotēs) has been addressed in dignity studies (Hicks, *Dignity*, 2011) — dignity as the inherent worth of persons, and dignified comportment as the visible expression of that worth." Dignity studies and virtue psychology (Peterson and Seligman's VIA framework) provide the primary lens. Social psychology's research on authority and status also illuminates the characteristic's household-governance (1Ti3:4) and teaching-authentication (Tit2:7) dimensions.

**[CHAR-6] Willing-heartedness** (E)

Per the science extract (wa-m09-humility-scienceextract-v1_0-20260513.md), the most relevant section is §3 (Submission, obedience, and deference), specifically the voluntary vs coerced submission distinction: "Voluntary submission (na.div — willing, noble-spirited) is categorically distinct from coerced compliance." Self-determination theory (SDT — Deci and Ryan) is also highly relevant: SDT distinguishes *intrinsic motivation* (acting from genuine inner interest and values) from *extrinsic motivation* (acting under external pressure or reward), which maps precisely onto the na.div (intrinsic) vs compelled-compliance (extrinsic) distinction. The science extract also identifies: "obedience arises from... trust in and agreement with the authority. Voluntary submission arises from genuine alignment of will — the person freely places themselves under an authority they trust and respect."

---

##### T7.3.2 — Where the human science framework illuminates the verse evidence — making a finding more coherent or complete — what does it reveal?

**[CHAR-1] Humility** (E)

The science framework illuminates several findings. (a) The genuine vs pseudo-humility distinction (Tangney): the Col2:18/23 critique of false humility maps precisely — false humility is identified in the science as self-abasement that is actually self-focused. The science framework explains *why* Col2:23 says performed asceticism "is of no value in stopping the indulgence of the flesh" — because self-focused performance keeps the self at the centre rather than genuinely reorienting toward others. (b) The epistemic-virtue dimension (T3.2.1 — humility enabling cognition): science research confirms the humility-wisdom connexion of Pro11:2 — intellectual humility (openness to information) is associated with better reasoning and learning outcomes, illuminating the biblical wisdom-principle empirically. (c) Situational vs dispositional humility (the science distinguishes): the corpus's distinction between the ka.na event-act (situational humbling) and tapeinofrosunē (dispositional humility) maps onto this distinction, confirming it is analytically real.

**[CHAR-2] Submission** (E)

The science framework illuminates three findings. (a) The M09-C/D split maps exactly onto the compliant-vs-internalised distinction: the "sincerity of heart" language (Eph6:5; Col3:22) that distinguishes genuine from eye-service submission is precisely the internalised-vs-compliant distinction in biblical terms. (b) Milgram's identification of proximity to authority and perceived legitimacy as determinants of compliance illuminates the Phili2:12 phenomenon from the reverse direction: Paul notes that the community must sustain obedience "much more in my absence" — the difficulty of obedience without authority-proximity is exactly what Milgram would predict; the biblical command to sustain it addresses this psychological tendency directly. (c) The voluntary submission (na.div — CHAR-6, distinct from this batch) / coerced compliance distinction in the science extract illuminates the biblical distinction between genuine submission (M09-C register) and the performed eye-service the corpus rejects.

**[CHAR-3] Contrition** (E)

The science framework's guilt-vs-shame distinction illuminates the characteristic's positive relational outcome (God's nearness and reviving — Psa34:18; Isa57:15): the research literature shows that *guilt* (action-focused moral remorse) is adaptive and leads toward repair and restored relationship, while *shame* (global self-condemnation) tends toward withdrawal and disconnection. The verse evidence evidences a characteristic that is action-focused (genuine confrontation with one's moral condition) and relationally adaptive (opening toward God's reviving), which is consistent with the guilt-profile rather than the shame-profile. This illuminates why God responds to contrition with nearness and reviving rather than with further condemnation — the contrite state in these verses is the guilt-adaptive form of moral self-confrontation rather than the shame-maladaptive form.

**[CHAR-4] Meekness and gentleness** (E)

The science framework illuminates two findings. (a) The empathy-through-shared-vulnerability mechanism (Heb5:2 — "he himself is beset with weakness" as the ground for metriopatheō): research on compassion and empathy confirms that genuine acquaintance with one's own suffering or limitation significantly enhances the capacity for compassionate engagement with others in their limitation — the high priest's shared-weakness ground is the biblical expression of the empirically confirmed empathy-through-vulnerability mechanism. (b) Self-regulation as the mechanism of metriopatheō's "measured" quality: Baumeister's self-regulation research illuminates why the characteristic requires active volitional governance — the impulse toward severity or detachment must be overridden by the measured-compassion response; self-regulatory capacity is what metriopatheō requires and names.

**[CHAR-5] Dignity** (E)

The science framework illuminates two findings. (a) Hicks's dignity studies: the distinction between *inherent dignity* (worth that belongs to a person simply as a human being) and *dignified comportment* (the visible expression of that worth) maps precisely onto the inner/outer structure of semnotēs evidenced across the three verses — the inner character quality (semnotēs as inner gravity) and its social expression (producing compliance, authenticating teaching, enabling peaceful life). (b) Social psychology's research on *authority through character vs authority through status* illuminates 1Ti3:4 — the overseer's dignity producing orderly compliance is precisely the character-based authority that social psychology identifies as more durably effective than status-based authority.

**[CHAR-6] Willing-heartedness** (E)

Self-determination theory illuminates three findings. (a) Exo35:5 — "whoever is of a willing heart, let him bring" — SDT's identification of intrinsic motivation as producing higher quality, more sustained, and more creative engagement maps onto the tabernacle-construction context: the willing-hearted craftsmen (1Ch28:21) bring their skill fully deployed, which SDT would predict as the characteristic behaviour of intrinsically-motivated persons. (b) Psa51:12 — SDT's research on the *internalisation* of external motivation confirms that the willing spirit after moral failure requires divine upholding (external support for renewed internalised motivation) — the science framework explains why the person cannot simply re-generate willing-heartedness through willpower after failure. (c) 2Ch29:31 — beyond-required offerings: SDT research confirms that intrinsically-motivated persons consistently exceed minimum compliance thresholds — the willing heart brings "burnt offerings" beyond what is required, which SDT would predict.

---

##### T7.3.3 — Where the verse evidence and the human science framework diverge, what does the divergence reveal?

**[CHAR-1] Humility** (E)

Per the science extract §T7.3.3 and §Outside-science inventory: the key divergence is the corpus's positive valorisation of *submission to God* as the highest expression of humility. The science framework (Milgram's obedience literature) treats submission to authority with deep suspicion — the primary lesson of compliance research is the danger of yielding to authority uncritically. The biblical corpus presents submission to God as constitutive of freedom and wisdom (Pro11:2; Mic6:8; Luk18:14), not as a form of pathological compliance. The divergence reveals that the character of the authority to whom one submits is *theologically determinative* in a way the social-science framework cannot account for: humbling before the righteous God who reverses the humble (Luk1:52) is categorically different from Milgram's obedience to an illegitimate experimental authority. The divergence surfaces the irreducibly theological dimension of the characteristic.

**[CHAR-2] Submission** (E)

The primary divergence is on the character of the authority being submitted to. The science framework (Milgram) treats submission to authority primarily as a social-psychological phenomenon with serious pathological risks — the dominant lesson is the danger of obedience. The biblical corpus presents obedience to God and legitimate authority as the highest expression of human freedom (Rom6:17 — transformation from sin's slavery to righteousness through heart-obedience) and the means of soul-purification (1Pe1:22). The divergence confirms the science extract's observation (§T7.3.3): "science addresses submission as a social compliance phenomenon; the biblical material addresses it as a relational reality constituted by the identity of the one submitted to." Willing submission to the righteous God is categorically different from Milgram's coerced compliance to an illegitimate experimental authority.

**[CHAR-3] Contrition** (E)

The primary divergence is on the *source and resolution* of the broken state. The science framework addresses contrition as a psychological phenomenon resolved through human repair processes (apology, amends, self-forgiveness). The biblical corpus evidences the resolution as God's direct action — "saves the crushed in spirit" (Psa34:18); "revives the spirit and heart" (Isa57:15) — without naming human repair processes. The divergence reveals that in the biblical corpus, contrition's resolution is not primarily self-directed or horizontally repaired but divinely revived: God is the agent of restoration, not the person themselves. This is categorically different from the science framework's emphasis on self-forgiveness and human moral repair.

**[CHAR-4] Meekness and gentleness** (E)

The science extract notes: "meekness in the biblical sense is not weakness but controlled strength — the capacity to restrain force when it could be used." The primary science divergence is on the *relationship between weakness and strength in this characteristic*. The science framework (self-regulation research) emphasises meekness as controlled strength — strength held back. Heb5:2 inverts this: the high priest's gentleness is grounded not in strength held back but in shared weakness honestly acknowledged. The biblical basis for metriopatheō is empathic vulnerability-awareness rather than self-regulatory strength. The divergence reveals that the biblical and science accounts of the characteristic's enabling ground differ: the science locates the ground in self-regulatory capacity (a form of strength); Heb5:2 locates it in shared creaturely weakness (a form of acknowledged limitation). Both produce calibrated gentleness, but from different constitutional sources.

**[CHAR-5] Dignity** (E)

The primary divergence is on the *source* of dignified comportment. The science framework (dignity studies) grounds dignity in inherent human worth — all persons have dignity by virtue of being human. The biblical corpus grounds semnotēs in *godliness* (1Ti2:2 — paired with eusebeia) and *integrity* (Tit2:7) — in genuine character formation in relation to God rather than in inherent human status. The divergence reveals that biblical semnotēs is a formed character quality rather than an inherent status-property — it is achieved through genuine godly formation rather than inherently possessed. This is theologically significant: the biblical account requires formation; the secular-dignitarian account does not.

**[CHAR-6] Willing-heartedness** (E)

The primary divergence is on the *ultimate source* of voluntary motivation. SDT grounds intrinsic motivation in the person's own psychological needs for autonomy, competence, and relatedness — it is fundamentally self-generated and self-sustaining (though supported by environmental conditions). The biblical corpus grounds the willing-hearted orientation ultimately in divine gift: Psa51:12 — "uphold me with a willing spirit" — the willing spirit requires divine sustaining, not merely environmental support. The divergence reveals that biblical willing-heartedness is not ultimately self-sustaining but divinely-dependent: the person may generate it naturally (VCG-01) but cannot regenerate it after moral failure without divine upholding. SDT does not account for the moral-failure disruption of intrinsic motivation that Psa51:12 addresses.

---

##### T7.3.4 — Does the human science framework surface any aspect of this characteristic that the verse evidence has not yet addressed — and does that absence require further verse investigation?

**[CHAR-1] Humility** (E)

The science framework surfaces the *developmental and hereditary* dimensions (extract §External influences — Developmental/Hereditary): parenting that gives honest proportionate feedback produces more accurate self-appraisal; secure attachment supports humility development; underlying traits (low narcissism, high openness) have some heritable components. The verse evidence corpus for CHAR-1 does not address developmental origins (how humility is formed in childhood or by parenting) or hereditary dimensions. This is a genuine evidence gap — the corpus is silent on the developmental formation of humility as a character trait. The gap does not require further verse investigation (the corpus genuinely does not address this register), but it is an important note for Session C publication: the developmental dimension of humility's formation is outside the Scripture corpus's address and is best addressed through cross-referencing with the science extract at publication stage.
