**[CHAR-2] Submission** (E)

Yes. The science framework's attention to *high power-distance vs low power-distance cultures* (Hofstede) surfaces a question the verse corpus does not directly address: whether the biblical submission vocabulary operates within and assumes a high power-distance cultural context (ancient Near East; Roman household codes) and whether its claims about inner submission are culturally bound to that context. The corpus does not directly address this — it assumes the authority structures it addresses without discussing their cultural relativity. This is a genuine gap for Session C presentation: cross-cultural readers from low power-distance contexts may receive the household submission texts (Eph6:1; Col3:20; 1Ti3:4) differently than high power-distance readers, and the programme's Phase 9 findings should note this as a reading-context variable.

**[CHAR-3] Contrition** (E)

The science framework surfaces the *phenomenology of the contrite inner state* as a gap: the two-verse corpus describes the state (crushed, broken) and God's response, but does not describe what the experience of contrition feels like from the inside — the phenomenological texture of the characteristic. Clinical psychology has developed rich descriptions of the phenomenology of genuine remorse (weight, grief, visceral discomfort, desire for repair) that the two-verse biblical corpus does not address. This is a genuine gap for Session C: the experiential description of contrition beyond its constitutional location (spirit, heart) and divine correlate (nearness, reviving) would benefit from the phenomenological contributions of clinical psychology.

**[CHAR-4] Meekness and gentleness** (E)

The science extract's note on *hereditary and temperamental dimensions* surfaces a gap: "temperamental impulsivity (lower self-regulatory capacity) has significant heritability." This implies that the okneō-absence quality (volitional readiness without hesitation) may have constitutional-temperamental components — some persons have higher baseline self-regulatory capacity and thus more readily manifest the characteristic. The biblical corpus does not address this hereditary or temperamental dimension. For Session C, this gap is worth noting: the characteristic's accessibility to different persons may vary by temperament in ways the biblical text does not explicitly address.

**[CHAR-5] Dignity** (E)

The science framework surfaces the *cultural relativity of dignified comportment* as a gap. What counts as "dignified" varies significantly across cultures (high power-distance vs low power-distance — Hofstede; the specific comportment norms differ). The three-verse corpus addresses semnotēs without specifying what its cross-cultural expressions look like — "sober, weighty seriousness" in a Mediterranean household context (1Ti3:4) may look quite different from semnotēs in other cultural contexts. For Session C, noting that the inner quality (settled moral gravity) is transcultural while its outward expression (the specific manner of dignified comportment) is culturally contextualised would be important for cross-cultural readers.

**[CHAR-6] Willing-heartedness** (E)

SDT's research on *conditions that support intrinsic motivation* (autonomy-supportive environments, acknowledgment of feelings, meaningful task context) surfaces a gap: the five-verse corpus calls for willing-heartedness and identifies God as its restorer, but does not directly address what community or environmental conditions support or undermine the willing-hearted orientation in normal (non-crisis) circumstances. The tabernacle and temple contexts (Exo35; 1Ch28:21; 2Ch29:31) implicitly show community-worship as the context for willing-heartedness, but the specific environmental conditions are not analysed. For Session C, noting that the science framework identifies conditions that support or undermine intrinsic motivation would be valuable context for applying the characteristic's insights to contemporary ministry and community life.
